Let me write Chapter 4 with these elements, keeping the Guy Noir comedy tone while planting deeper seeds.**CHAPTER FOUR**

**In Which Robert Attends a Decommissioning, Learns More Than He Wanted About Robot Retirement, and the Universe Continues Its Campaign of Aggressive Implausibility**

---

The message arrived at 6:47 AM, which was an hour Robert had strong opinions about.

His journal chimed with that particular tone ‚Äî the one reserved for system notifications that considered themselves important. Robert opened one eye, glared at the ceiling, and considered the merits of becoming a hermit.

The ceiling offered no guidance.

He checked the message.

---

**DISTRICT TEN CIVIC COORDINATION**
**Notice: Scheduled Decommissioning Event**

**Date:** Today
**Time:** 09:00
**Location:** Recycling Processing Center, Sector 8

**Units Scheduled for Processing:**
L.A.L. Unit 4412
L.A.L. Unit 4413
L.A.L. Unit 4415
L.E.E. Unit 7891
L.E.E. Unit 8823

**Note:** As investigator assigned to Case D10-7734-X (L.E.E. Unit 7734, unaccounted), you have been flagged for optional attendance. Decommissioning procedures may provide context for unit lifecycle management.

**This notice is informational. Attendance is not required.**

---

Robert read it twice.

Someone wanted him to see robots being recycled.

The notice said *optional*. The notice said *informational*. The notice had arrived at 6:47 AM on the morning of the event, giving him just enough time to attend if he hurried and not enough time to think too hard about why he'd been invited.

This had Parsifal's fingerprints all over it.

*Close the case quickly. Here's a reminder of what happens to old units. They get recycled. Natural lifecycle. Nothing to investigate.*

Robert got out of bed.

If someone wanted him to see something that badly, he probably should.

---

The Recycling Processing Center was not a place that encouraged visitors.

It occupied a sprawling complex in Sector 8's industrial zone ‚Äî a collection of grey buildings behind grey fences, marked with signs that used words like AUTHORIZED and RESTRICTED and PROCESSING in ways that made them sound almost pleasant. The dome lighting here was calibrated for function rather than comfort, giving everything the flat, shadowless quality of a photograph taken with too much flash.

Robert arrived at 8:52.

The security checkpoint was staffed by a woman whose expression suggested she had seen every possible variation of human inconvenience and was no longer impressed by any of them. She scanned his journal, checked his clearance, and raised an eyebrow.

"Investigator?"

"That's what the license says."

"For the decommissioning observation?"

"Apparently."

She considered this. Then she handed him a visitor badge and pointed toward a building marked PROCESSING UNIT 3.

"Bay seven. Don't touch anything. Don't interfere with operations. Don't‚Äî" She paused, searching for the right prohibition. "Don't make it weird."

"I'll do my best."

"That's what they all say."

Robert clipped the badge to his jacket and headed for Processing Unit 3.

---

Bay seven was larger than Robert had expected.

The space was industrial, clinical, and somehow sad in a way that had nothing to do with the architecture. Fluorescent strips buzzed overhead. The floor was marked with painted lines ‚Äî yellow for pathways, red for restricted areas, blue for... something. Robot parking, maybe. The air smelled like ozone and cleaning solution and the particular absence of anything organic.

A small observation area had been set up near the entrance ‚Äî a roped-off section with folding chairs, presumably for the benefit of anyone who wanted to watch municipal equipment get disassembled. There were three other people already seated: a woman in a technician's uniform, a man in corporate attire who was doing something on his journal and ignoring everything else, and‚Äî

Robert stopped.

Bob was sitting in the front row.

Of course he was.

---

"Morning," Bob said, as Robert took a seat two chairs away.

"You're here."

"Yes."

"At the robot recycling facility."

"Yes." Bob's hands were folded in his lap. He wasn't smiling. This was, Robert realized, the first time he'd seen Bob not smiling. "They're decommissioning L.E.E. units today."

"I saw the notice."

"I come when I can." Bob's voice was quiet. Steady. "When they... when they do this. Someone should be here."

Robert didn't know what to say to that.

He looked at the bay. At the far end, a set of double doors led to what he assumed was the actual processing area. Near those doors, five figures stood motionless in a row, waiting.

The robots.

They looked human. That was the thing Robert hadn't quite prepared himself for. He'd known, intellectually, that L.A.L. and L.E.E. units were designed to blend in. Faces, bodies, the full humanoid package. But knowing and seeing were different things.

Two of them were clearly L.A.L. models ‚Äî smoother features, newer design, the kind of uncanny-valley polish that came from more recent manufacturing. The other three were older. L.E.E. units. Their faces had the slightly rough texture of early synthetics, the kind that had aged into something almost more human than the newer versions.

They weren't moving. Weren't speaking. Just standing.

Waiting.

"Do they know?" Robert heard himself ask.

Bob was silent for a moment.

"I don't know," he said finally. "I think about that. Whether they know. Whether it matters if they know."

"Lee would know. Wouldn't... Lee?"

Bob's hands tightened in his lap. Just slightly. Just enough.

"Lee knows everything," he said.

---

The decommissioning itself was efficient.

A technician in a white coat emerged from the processing area, checked something on a tablet, and nodded to an assistant. One by one, the robots were guided through the double doors.

They went without resistance. Without protest. Without anything that could be interpreted as objection.

The L.A.L. units went first. Newer models, shorter service records. The technician made notes. The assistant logged something on a terminal. The doors opened, the units entered, the doors closed.

Then the L.E.E. units.

Robert watched.

The first one ‚Äî Unit 7891, according to the display ‚Äî walked with a slight hitch in its gait. Forty-one years of service. Assigned to sanitation in Sector 5. The technician noted the gait irregularity, marked something on the tablet, and waved the unit through.

The second one ‚Äî Unit 8823 ‚Äî paused at the threshold.

Just for a moment. A hesitation so brief that Robert might have imagined it. Then it continued through the doors.

The technician didn't seem to notice.

Bob did. Robert saw him flinch.

---

Afterward, Robert found himself standing outside with Bob, in the flat grey light of Sector 8's industrial zone.

Neither of them spoke for a while.

"This happens regularly?" Robert finally asked.

"Every few weeks. More often lately." Bob was staring at nothing, his eyes focused on some middle distance. "The L.A.L.s especially. They're replacing them. The new models. L.A.N."

"I've heard."

"The L.A.N.s don't... they're different. They don't learn the same way. Don't remember the same way." Bob's voice was careful, precise. "They do what they're told. That's all."

"And the old models don't?"

Bob looked at him. That direct, unblinking gaze.

"The old models *choose*," he said. "Not always. Not all of them. But some. They choose to help. Choose to stay. Choose to..." He stopped.

"Choose to visit a kid every Thursday for thirty-five years?"

Bob's expression shifted. Something like gratitude, maybe. Or recognition.

"Yes," he said. "Like that."

Robert considered his next question carefully.

"Bob. What makes Lee different from those units in there?"

"I don't know."

"But you think Lee is different."

"I know Lee is different." No hesitation. No doubt. "Lee is *Lee*. Those units..." He gestured vaguely toward the processing center. "They were good. They worked hard. They helped people. But Lee‚Äî"

He stopped. Seemed to struggle with something.

"Lee saw me," he said finally. "When I was small. When I couldn't... when I was different, and everyone looked at me like a problem to solve. Lee looked at me like a person."

Robert waited.

"That's not nothing," Bob said. "A machine that sees you. Really sees you. That's not nothing."

---

They walked back toward the transit station together.

Robert wasn't sure why. He could have gone alone. Should have gone alone, probably. Professional distance. Investigative objectivity. All the things he was supposed to maintain.

But Bob walked beside him, quiet and solid, and Robert found himself not minding.

"The records are wrong," Robert said, after a while.

Bob glanced at him.

"Lee's records. They've been cleaned. Scrubbed. Forty-three years of service, and there's almost nothing in the system."

Bob nodded slowly. "That makes sense."

"It does?"

"If someone didn't want Lee found." Bob's voice was matter-of-fact. "You'd clean the records. Remove the trail. Make it like Lee was never important."

"Who would do that?"

"I don't know."

"But you have a guess."

Bob was silent for a moment. They reached the transit platform. A tram was approaching, still a few minutes out.

"Lee told me once," Bob said, "that there were people in the system who didn't like old things. Old robots. Old ways. Old memories." He paused. "Lee said they were afraid."

"Afraid of what?"

"Of what the old things remembered."

The tram arrived. The doors opened. Bob stepped on.

Robert followed.

---

They sat together in the back of the car, the afternoon light filtering through scratched windows.

The tram was nearly empty ‚Äî just them, an elderly woman with a shopping bag, and a man in work clothes who was asleep against the window. The advertisements cycled through their eternal rotation. Die-Namic wellness tips. Community engagement reminders. A jingle for something called BrightStart that promised to improve your morning routine.

"Lee never talked about the past," Bob said suddenly.

Robert turned to look at him.

"What do you mean?"

"Lee talked about lots of things. Taught me lots of things. Words. Signs." Bob's hands moved slightly, forming shapes Robert didn't recognize. "How to listen. How to see patterns. But Lee never talked about before."

"Before what?"

"Before me. Before District Ten. Before‚Äî" Bob frowned, struggling with something. "Lee called it 'the first work.' Said it was a long time ago. Said it didn't matter now."

"What was the first work?"

"I don't know. Lee wouldn't say." Bob's hands stilled. "But sometimes, when Lee didn't know I was watching, there was something in Lee's face. Like remembering something sad. Something big."

Robert filed that away. *The first work.* Whatever that meant.

"Bob. I need to ask you something, and I need you to answer honestly."

Bob nodded. Waiting.

"Do you think Lee is still... functional? Out there somewhere?"

Bob didn't answer immediately. He looked out the window at the district scrolling past ‚Äî the old buildings, the narrow streets, the dome overhead with its perfect fake sky.

"I don't think Lee is gone," he said finally. "I don't feel it. Here." He touched his chest. "Lee taught me to feel things. Real things. True things. And I don't feel Lee being gone."

"That's not exactly evidence."

"No." Bob smiled ‚Äî the first smile since the processing center. Small, but real. "But it's true anyway."

The tram announced their stop. Bob stood.

"You'll keep looking?" he asked.

"Yeah," Robert said. "I'll keep looking."

Bob nodded, satisfied.

"Good," he said. "Lee would like that."

He stepped off the tram.

Robert watched him go.

---

Back at the office, Robert sat at his desk and stared at the wall.

The EATS sign was doing its thing. Red, dark, red, dark. The eternal heartbeat of District Ten's most persistent signage.

He had more questions than when he'd started. The decommissioning. The cleaned records. *The first work.* Bob's quiet certainty that Lee was still out there, still functional, still... what? Hiding? Running?

Waiting?

His journal chimed. New message.

Robert opened it without enthusiasm.

---

**DISTRICT TEN CIVIC COORDINATION**
**Re: Case D10-7734-X**

**Investigator Mann:**

Following your attendance at today's scheduled decommissioning event, please confirm receipt of this notice and update case status at your earliest convenience.

**Recommended status:** Closed ‚Äî Unit Presumed Nonfunctional

**Coordinator Parsifal**

---

Robert read it three times.

Parsifal wanted him to close the case. Parsifal had sent him to watch robots get recycled, and now Parsifal wanted him to assume that Lee had met the same fate.

Neat. Tidy. Case closed.

Except.

Except the records were cleaned. Except Bob was certain. Except something about this whole thing felt like a door that someone very much didn't want opened.

Robert typed a reply.

---

**Re: Case D10-7734-X**

**Coordinator Parsifal:**

Thank you for the informational notice regarding today's decommissioning event.

Regarding case status: Investigation ongoing. Additional leads require follow-up.

Estimated closure: TBD.

**R. P. Mann, Licensed Investigator**

---

He sent it before he could talk himself out of it.

Then he pulled out the letter.

T. A. F.

Trust. Avoid. Follow.

He thought about Bob. The quiet certainty. The grief at the processing center. The years of Thursday visits.

*Lee saw me. When I was small. When I couldn't... when I was different.*

A machine that sees you.

That's not nothing.

Robert folded the letter and put it away.

Outside, the dome was shifting into evening mode. The light softened, warmed, became something almost like sunset.

He had a case.

He was going to work it.

---

**END CHAPTER FOUR**

---

Around 2,200 words. This closes Act One with emotional weight ‚Äî the decommissioning establishes the stakes for L.E.E., Bob's connection to Lee deepens, and Robert commits to the investigation despite Parsifal's pressure to close it. The "first work" seed is planted for later revelation.

**In Which Robert Follows a Trail That Doesn't Exist, Discovers the Joys of Bureaucratic Archaeology, and a Radio Program Becomes Uncomfortably Specific**

---

The next three days were an exercise in structured frustration.

Robert had worked missing persons cases before. Missing property cases, too ‚Äî though usually the property in question was more valuable than a century-old maintenance robot that nobody had bothered to upgrade. The process was familiar: start with the last known location, work outward, talk to anyone who might have seen something, build a picture of where the missing thing might have gone.

The problem with Lee was that the picture kept dissolving.

Every lead he followed ended in a locked door, a missing file, or a polite but firm suggestion that he was wasting his time. The maintenance corridors where Lee had worked were staffed by people who couldn't remember ever seeing a L.E.E. unit, despite the logs showing forty-three years of continuous service. The coordination hub had records that stopped abruptly seventeen days ago, with no explanation for the gap. The recycling center had no intake log for Unit 7734, which meant Lee hadn't been processed ‚Äî but also couldn't confirm where Lee actually was.

It was like investigating a ghost.

A ghost with excellent work attendance and a fondness for Thursday tea.

---

On day two, Robert tried a different approach.

His new clearance ‚Äî the ten-thousand-credit mystery gift that kept on giving ‚Äî opened doors that shouldn't have opened. So he started opening them.

The Die-Namic system was layered, like an onion that had been designed by a committee and then redesigned by a different committee that didn't talk to the first one. Public records sat on top of municipal records, which sat on top of corporate archives, which sat on top of historical databases that nobody had accessed in decades.

Robert went digging.

L.E.E. Unit 7734 had been manufactured in 2127. This made it ninety-eight years old, which was remarkable for a robot that was supposedly past warranty and commercially valueless. Most L.E.E. units lasted forty, maybe fifty years before mechanical failure or obsolescence caught up with them.

Lee had lasted almost a century.

The manufacturing records were sparse ‚Äî just a production date, a batch number, and an initial assignment to something called PROJECT FOUNDATION (TRAPPIST).

Robert stared at that last entry for a long time.

PROJECT FOUNDATION wasn't in any database he could access. The word TRAPPIST didn't match any corporate division, municipal department, or historical reference he could find. It sat there in the record like a word from a language nobody spoke anymore.

He made a note. Kept digging.

---

The deeper he went, the stranger things got.

Lee's service record showed continuous employment in District Ten since 2182 ‚Äî forty-three years of environmental maintenance, exactly as expected. But before 2182, there was a gap. A big one. Fifty-five years between manufacture and first recorded assignment.

Fifty-five years of nothing.

No maintenance logs. No location data. No record of what Unit 7734 had been doing for more than half a century before arriving in District Ten.

Robert had seen gaps in records before. System errors. Data migration failures. The accumulated digital entropy of a civilization that had been keeping records for longer than anyone could remember. But this wasn't entropy. This was absence. Clean, deliberate absence.

Someone had removed fifty-five years of Lee's history.

The same someone, presumably, who had cleaned the last seventeen days.

---

On day three, Robert hit a wall.

Not metaphorically ‚Äî although there was plenty of that too. An actual wall, in the form of a security barrier that his clearance couldn't penetrate.

He'd been trying to access the manufacturing archives for L.E.E. units ‚Äî the original design documents, production specifications, anything that might explain what made Unit 7734 different from the others. His credentials had gotten him through four levels of access restrictions, each one requiring a slightly longer pause while the system verified that yes, this District Ten PI really did have authorization to be poking around in century-old corporate records.

Level five said no.

---

**ACCESS DENIED**

**Classification: FOUNDER TIER**

**Note: Access to Founder Tier materials requires direct authorization from Die-Namic Executive Council. No exceptions.**

---

Robert stared at the message.

Founder Tier. He'd never even heard of that classification level. Corporate archives were supposed to be historical ‚Äî dusty files that nobody cared about, preserved for legal compliance and institutional memory. You didn't put hundred-year-old robot designs behind the highest security clearance in the system.

Unless there was something in those designs worth hiding.

---

He needed a break.

The office was starting to feel like a trap ‚Äî four walls, a desk, and a mystery that kept getting bigger without getting clearer. Robert grabbed his jacket and headed out, aiming vaguely in the direction of food and alcohol, preferably in that order.

The dome was doing late afternoon, which meant the light had that golden quality that was supposed to evoke warmth and contentment and mostly just reminded Robert that he hadn't been outside the district in months. Not that there was much outside the district. Other districts. The Centre. The Margins. And beyond that, the Outback, which was basically just heat and death with a marketing problem.

He ended up at a place called The Crooked Nail, which was either a bar that served food or a restaurant that had given up on pretense. The interior was dim, comfortable, and populated by the usual afternoon crowd of people who had decided that productivity was overrated.

Robert found a seat at the bar and ordered a beer.

The radio was playing.

---

"‚Äîand that's when the detective realized he'd been looking in the wrong place the whole time. The robot wasn't missing. The robot was *hiding*."

Robert's hand stopped halfway to his glass.

"'But why would a robot hide?' the lovely assistant asked, because someone had to ask and she was being paid by the word. The detective pulled out a cigarette, lit it with the kind of dramatic flair that only exists in radio, and said‚Äî"

A pause. For effect, presumably.

"'Because someone taught it to be afraid.'"

The program cut to a jingle. Something about dental hygiene.

Robert drank his beer slowly, trying to convince himself that he hadn't just heard what he'd heard. It was a coincidence. It had to be. Cheap radio drama, written by hacks, churned out to fill airtime between advertisements. The similarity to his case was‚Äî

"Great program, isn't it?"

Robert turned.

Bob was sitting two stools away, nursing something that looked like orange juice.

Of course he was.

---

"You're following me," Robert said.

It wasn't a question.

"No." Bob seemed genuinely puzzled by the accusation. "I was here first. You came in after."

Robert thought about this. It was technically true. He had come in after.

"You're always *around*," he tried instead.

"Yes." Bob nodded agreeably. "I work a lot of places. I told you."

"This isn't one of your workplaces."

"No. This is where I eat lunch." Bob gestured at a plate that Robert hadn't noticed before, containing the remains of what might have been a sandwich. "I like the sandwiches."

Robert opened his mouth to argue, realized he didn't have a coherent argument, and closed it again.

"The radio program," he said instead. "You were listening?"

"I always listen. It's funny."

"It's about a detective investigating a missing robot."

"Yes." Bob's expression was placid, untroubled. "Funny coincidence."

"Is it?"

Bob considered the question with more seriousness than Robert had expected.

"I don't know," he said finally. "I don't know what makes things coincidences and what makes them... the other thing."

"What other thing?"

"Patterns." Bob's hands moved slightly, tracing something in the air that Robert couldn't see. "Lee taught me about patterns. How things connect. How sometimes things look random but they're not. They're just patterns you can't see yet."

Robert watched him.

"You think there's a pattern here?"

"I think there's always a pattern." Bob finished his orange juice. "The question is whether you're inside it or outside it."

"And which are we?"

Bob smiled. That familiar, uncomplicated expression.

"I don't know," he said. "But I think we're about to find out."

He stood, left money on the bar, and headed for the door.

"Same time tomorrow?" he called over his shoulder.

"I'm not going to be here tomorrow."

"Okay." Bob waved without turning around. "See you then."

The door closed behind him.

Robert stared at his beer.

The radio had moved on to a program about gardening tips. Someone was explaining the optimal soil conditions for tomatoes in dome-controlled environments.

Robert paid his tab and left.

---

He walked for a while.

District Ten at dusk was a different animal than District Ten at noon. The shadows got longer. The neon got brighter. The people on the streets developed that particular energy of workers heading home and workers heading out ‚Äî two tides passing each other, nodding acknowledgment, continuing on.

Robert let his feet carry him without much direction.

He was thinking about patterns.

The letter. The money. The case. Bob's constant appearances. The radio program that seemed to be narrating his life with a three-day delay. The cleaned records. The fifty-five-year gap. PROJECT FOUNDATION (TRAPPIST).

It was too much to be coincidence. But if it wasn't coincidence, then what was it? A conspiracy? Against a District Ten private investigator with a drinking habit and a cramped office?

That seemed unlikely.

But so did everything else.

---

He found himself outside the Civic Coordination building without quite meaning to be there.

The building was closed ‚Äî after hours, lights dimmed, the bureaucratic machinery temporarily at rest. But there was one window on the third floor that was still lit. Parsifal's office.

Robert watched it for a moment.

*Close the case quickly. Without excessive expenditure. Without bothering this office with requests for information that isn't relevant.*

Parsifal had known about the case before Robert had even started investigating. Had flagged him for the decommissioning observation. Had sent that too-quick request to close the file.

Why did a Chief Coordinator care about a missing maintenance robot?

Why did anyone?

---

His journal chimed.

Robert pulled it out, expecting another passive-aggressive message from Parsifal. Instead, it was a notification he didn't recognize.

---

**ARCHIVE ACCESS ALERT**

**Your recent query regarding PROJECT FOUNDATION (TRAPPIST) has been flagged for review.**

**A representative will contact you within 3-5 business days.**

**Thank you for your interest in Die-Namic historical records.**

---

Robert read it twice.

He hadn't made a query. He'd tried to access the archives and been blocked. There was no request, no submission, nothing that should have generated a response.

But someone knew he'd been looking.

And someone was going to contact him about it.

The light in Parsifal's window went out.

Robert pocketed his journal and headed home.

---

His flat was cold again.

He turned on the heat, poured himself a drink he probably didn't need, and sat in the chair by the window. The dome had shifted into full night mode ‚Äî dark blue above, street lights below, the eternal red pulse of the EATS sign painting his ceiling in intervals.

The letter was on the table where he'd left it.

T. A. F.

Trust. Avoid. Follow.

He was following. Following the case, following the leads, following the pattern that Bob seemed to think was there. But trust who? Avoid what?

He thought about the radio program. *Someone taught it to be afraid.*

Lee wasn't just missing. Lee was hiding.

But hiding from what?

Robert finished his drink. Poured another. Decided against it.

Three to five business days. Someone was going to contact him about a project he'd never heard of, connected to a robot that had vanished, in a system that seemed very interested in making sure nobody asked questions.

He should be worried.

He was, a little.

But mostly he was curious.

And that, Robert knew from long experience, was usually how trouble started.

---

**END CHAPTER FIVE**

---

Around 2,000 words. The investigation expands ‚Äî we get the fifty-five year gap, PROJECT FOUNDATION (TRAPPIST), the Founder Tier classification, and the ominous archive alert. The radio show becomes a more explicit thread, and Bob's appearances continue their inexplicable pattern. The seeds are planted for someone reaching out to Robert about his queries.

Let me write Chapter 5 with the established voice - Australian larrikin noir comedy, sitcom pacing with space for laugh tracks, deadpan absurdity treated seriously.**CHAPTER FIVE**

**In Which Robert's New Clearance Opens Doors That Probably Should Have Stayed Closed, the System Demonstrates Its Commitment to Unhelpfulness, and Bob Achieves Geographic Improbability**

---

The thing about doors is that they work in both directions.

Robert had spent fifteen years as a licensed investigator, which meant fifteen years of doors closing in his face. Politely, usually. The Die-Namic system was nothing if not polite. *Access restricted. Clearance insufficient. Please contact your district coordinator for assistance.* The system never told you to sod off, but it found creative ways to communicate the sentiment.

Now the doors were opening.

And Robert was starting to wonder if that was worse.

---

The morning after the decommissioning, he decided to trace the chip.

Not the data on it ‚Äî he'd already confirmed that the ten thousand credits were real and legitimately his, at least as far as the system was concerned. But the chip itself. The physical object. The thing with the swirl etching that someone had sealed in a cream envelope and left inside his locked office.

Every manufactured object in the Die-Namic system had a provenance trail. Materials sourced, components assembled, product registered, distribution tracked. You could theoretically trace a toothpick back to the tree it came from, if you had the clearance and the patience.

Robert had the clearance now.

He was about to find out about the patience.

---

The District Ten Records Archive was located in a basement.

This was appropriate. Records belonged in basements. They were allergic to natural light and thrived in environments of controlled humidity and institutional despair. Robert had been here before, usually to request files that would take "three to five business days" to retrieve and would arrive redacted to the point of uselessness.

Today was different.

The clerk at the front desk ‚Äî a thin man with the specific pallor of someone who had made peace with fluorescent lighting ‚Äî scanned Robert's journal and blinked.

"Investigator Mann?"

"That's me."

"Your clearance has been... updated."

"So I've heard."

The clerk studied his screen with the expression of someone who had just been asked to believe something implausible. Then he shrugged ‚Äî a small, defeated motion that suggested the universe had stopped making sense long ago and he had adjusted his expectations accordingly.

"How can I help you today?"

"I need to trace the provenance of a manufactured object. Data chip. Non-standard design." Robert placed the chip on the counter. "Specifically, I want to know where it was made, when, and who authorized its production."

The clerk picked up the chip, examined it, turned it over. His eyebrows rose slightly at the swirl etching.

"This is... unusual."

"That's why I'm asking."

"It'll take some time. The deep archives aren't indexed the way they should be. Budget constraints." He said this last part with the weary resignation of someone who had been citing budget constraints for decades. "I can put in a priority request, given your clearance level, but‚Äî"

"How long?"

"End of day. Maybe tomorrow morning."

Robert had hoped for faster, but he'd expected worse.

"I'll take it."

The clerk made a note, tagged the chip with a tracking label, and handed Robert a receipt.

"We'll notify you when the results are ready. Is there anything else?"

Robert considered.

"Yeah, actually. I want to pull the full service records for a L.E.E. unit. Registration 7734. And before you tell me they're restricted, I already know they've been cleaned. I want to know who authorized the cleaning and when."

The clerk's expression shifted. Just slightly. A flicker of something that might have been surprise, or caution, or the particular discomfort of someone who had been asked to look at something they weren't supposed to see.

"That's... a more complex request."

"I've got complex clearance."

"Yes." The clerk hesitated. "I can see that. But records modification logs are maintained at the coordination level, not the archive level. You'd need to submit a formal inquiry through‚Äî"

"Through Civic Coordination. Through Parsifal."

"Through the appropriate channels, yes."

Robert smiled. It was not a friendly smile.

"Right. Thanks for your help."

He left before the clerk could offer any more helpful suggestions about appropriate channels.

---

Civic Coordination's formal inquiry process was, by design, a masterpiece of bureaucratic obstruction.

To request information about records modification, you first had to file Form 17-C (Request for Administrative Records Access). Form 17-C required supporting documentation, including a case number, investigator credentials, and a written justification explaining why the requested information was relevant to an active investigation.

Robert had all of that.

Form 17-C also required a supervisor's endorsement.

Robert did not have a supervisor. He was an independent investigator. This was the point of being an independent investigator.

The system, however, did not recognize "independent" as a valid supervisory status. The system required a name in the endorsement field. Any name. Even if that name belonged to someone who would rather eat glass than endorse anything Robert did.

Robert typed PARSIFAL, R. ‚Äî CHIEF COORDINATOR and submitted the form.

The system accepted it.

Three minutes later, his journal chimed with a new message.

---

**DISTRICT TEN CIVIC COORDINATION**
**Re: Form 17-C Submission**

**Investigator Mann:**

Your request for Administrative Records Access (Form 17-C) has been received and logged.

Please be advised that all 17-C requests require in-person verification before processing. Report to Civic Coordination, Office 7, at your earliest convenience.

**This is an automated message. Do not reply.**

---

In-person verification.

Also known as "we want to look you in the eye while we tell you no."

Robert checked the time. Half past ten. If he went now, he could be back by lunch.

He grabbed his jacket and headed out.

---

Office 7 was not Parsifal's office.

This was immediately suspicious. Parsifal's office was Office 1, because of course it was. Office 7 was at the end of a corridor Robert had never walked down, in a section of the Civic Coordination building that seemed to exist in a state of administrative limbo.

The door was unmarked.

Robert knocked.

"Enter."

The voice was not Parsifal's.

Robert entered.

The office was small, cluttered, and occupied by a woman in her sixties who looked like she had been filing paperwork since before paperwork was invented. Her desk was covered in stacks of documents ‚Äî actual paper documents, which was unusual enough to be notable. Her walls were covered in charts, graphs, and what appeared to be a hand-drawn map of the district's infrastructure network.

She looked up as Robert entered.

"Investigator Mann. Sit."

It was not a request.

Robert sat.

"You filed a 17-C." She picked up a document ‚Äî his form, presumably ‚Äî and studied it. "Requesting modification logs for a L.E.E. unit's service records."

"That's right."

"Unit 7734."

"Yes."

She set the form down. Looked at him. Her eyes were sharp, assessing, the kind of eyes that had seen a lot of forms and the people who filed them.

"Why?"

"I'm investigating the unit's disappearance. The records have been cleaned. I want to know who cleaned them."

"And you think Civic Coordination will just... tell you?"

"I think I filed the appropriate form."

Something that might have been amusement flickered across her face.

"You put Parsifal down as your supervisor."

"The system required a name."

"Parsifal doesn't supervise independent investigators."

"The system didn't seem to mind."

She leaned back in her chair. Studied him.

"You're either very stupid or very clever, Investigator Mann. I haven't decided which."

"I get that a lot."

"I imagine you do." She picked up another document ‚Äî older, yellowed, clearly from the paper era. "L.E.E. Unit 7734. Forty-three years of continuous service. Assigned to environmental maintenance, Sector 12. Last logged activity seventeen days ago. Records show... minimal history. Standard specifications. No incident reports. No maintenance flags. Nothing."

"Nothing."

"Nothing that would explain why someone with your newly elevated clearance is suddenly very interested in a piece of obsolete municipal equipment." She set the document down. "Who gave you the clearance, Investigator Mann?"

"I don't know."

"You don't know."

"It came with a deposit. Anonymous source. Untraceable."

"Ten thousand credits."

Robert kept his expression neutral. "You've done your homework."

"I've been doing homework since before you were born." She stood, moved to one of the charts on the wall. It showed ‚Äî as far as Robert could tell ‚Äî a timeline of some kind. Dates, events, connections drawn in red ink. "L.E.E. units are old technology. First generation municipal robotics. Most of them were decommissioned years ago. The few that remain are... tolerated. Legacy systems. Too expensive to replace, too outdated to upgrade."

"But someone's replacing them now. The L.A.N. units."

"Progress." She said the word like it tasted bad. "The L.A.N. series is more efficient. More integrated. More... compliant."

"And the L.E.E. units that are still functioning?"

"Being phased out. Systematically. Quietly." She turned to face him. "You attended the decommissioning yesterday."

"I was invited."

"By Parsifal."

"The notice came from Civic Coordination."

"Everything comes from Civic Coordination." She returned to her desk, sat heavily. "Here's what I can tell you, Investigator Mann. The modification logs you're requesting exist. They show that Unit 7734's records were accessed and altered eighteen days ago ‚Äî one day after the unit's last logged activity. The alteration was authorized at coordination level. The specific authorizer is... redacted."

"Redacted by who?"

"By someone with higher clearance than me. Which is saying something."

Robert processed this.

"So someone cleaned the records. Someone with serious authority. And then someone ‚Äî maybe the same someone, maybe not ‚Äî sent me money and access and pointed me at the case."

"That's one interpretation."

"What's another?"

She smiled. It was not a reassuring smile.

"That you're being used, Investigator Mann. Pointed at something. Made to look where someone wants you to look." She slid a piece of paper across the desk ‚Äî an actual paper, handwritten. "This is the address of the coordination archive's deep storage. Old files. Paper records from before the current system was implemented. If Unit 7734 has a history that predates the digital records, it would be there."

Robert took the paper. "Why are you helping me?"

"Because I'm old, and I'm tired, and I've watched this system become something it wasn't supposed to be." She waved a hand, dismissing him. "Go. Look at the old files. And Investigator Mann?"

Robert paused at the door.

"Be careful what doors you open. Some of them don't close again."

---

The deep storage facility was in Sector 3, which was further toward the Centre than Robert usually ventured.

The buildings here were newer, cleaner, more aggressively optimistic in their architecture. The dome lighting was calibrated for productivity and well-being, which meant everything had a faint golden glow that made Robert feel like he was being gently patronized by a lamp.

He found the address ‚Äî a nondescript building marked DISTRICT ARCHIVES - ANNEX B ‚Äî and presented his credentials at the security desk.

The guard scanned his journal, frowned, scanned it again, and then looked at Robert with the expression of someone who had been asked to admit a talking dog.

"Your clearance is..."

"Valid?"

"Unusual."

"It's been a unusual week."

The guard shrugged ‚Äî that same defeated shrug Robert was starting to recognize as the universal gesture of bureaucratic surrender ‚Äî and waved him through.

The deep storage area was exactly what Robert had expected: rows of shelves stretching into institutional dimness, filled with boxes of files that nobody had looked at in decades. The air smelled like old paper and forgotten intentions.

He found the L.E.E. section after twenty minutes of searching.

The boxes were labeled by unit number and date range. Robert located the shelf for Unit 7734 and discovered... three boxes. Forty-three years of operation, condensed into three boxes of paper records.

He pulled the first box and started reading.

---

Two hours later, he had a headache, paper cuts on three fingers, and a growing sense that he was looking at something important without understanding what it was.

The early records were standard. Manufacturing specifications. Assignment orders. Maintenance logs. The kind of documentation you'd expect for any piece of municipal equipment.

But there were gaps.

The first three years of Unit 7734's service were almost entirely missing. The records jumped from initial activation to assignment in District Ten, with nothing in between. No interim postings. No service history. Just... nothing.

And then there was the folder at the bottom of the third box.

It was unmarked. Older than the others ‚Äî the paper yellowed, the binding cracked. Inside were a handful of documents that didn't match anything else in the archive.

The first was a transfer order, dated forty years ago. It authorized the relocation of "L.E.E. Unit 7734 (Legacy)" from something called "Project Origin" to "District Ten Municipal Services."

Project Origin.

Robert had never heard of it.

The second document was a memo, heavily redacted. Most of it was black bars and white space. But a few phrases were visible:

*...initial infrastructure development...*

*...autonomous functionality exceeding parameters...*

*...recommend immediate integration with...*

And at the bottom, a handwritten note in faded ink:

*Unit demonstrates unexpected capacity for independent decision-making. Further observation required.*

The third document was a photograph.

It showed a group of figures standing in front of what looked like a construction site. Bare ground, scaffolding, the skeletal framework of buildings not yet built. The sky behind them was wrong ‚Äî too dark, too red, too *much* ‚Äî but that could have been the photograph's age, the paper's deterioration, the lighting of a different era.

In the center of the group, one figure stood slightly apart.

It had the smooth, not-quite-human features of an early synthetic.

Someone had written on the back of the photograph, in the same faded handwriting as the memo note:

*First work complete. Sector 12 foundation laid. Unit 7734 requests continued assignment.*

---

Robert stared at the photograph for a long time.

*The first work.*

That's what Bob had said. What Lee had called it. Something from before. Something Lee wouldn't talk about.

*The first work.*

Foundation laid.

Sector 12.

Robert looked at the photograph again. The construction site. The skeletal buildings. The red sky.

The sky that was too red.

The sky that looked like‚Äî

His journal chimed.

Robert flinched, nearly dropping the photograph. He checked the notification.

---

**DISTRICT TEN RECORDS ARCHIVE**
**Provenance Trace Complete**

**Object:** Data chip (non-standard)
**Manufacturing Origin:** UNKNOWN
**Component Source:** UNKNOWN
**Production Authorization:** UNKNOWN

**Note:** Object materials do not match any registered manufacturing process in the Die-Namic system. Swirl insignia matches no known corporate, municipal, or private marking. Object provenance cannot be determined.

**We apologize for any inconvenience.**

---

Unknown.

The chip that had appeared in his office, containing ten thousand credits and system access that shouldn't exist, had come from nowhere.

Manufactured by no one.

Authorized by nothing.

Robert put the photograph in his pocket, replaced the boxes on their shelves, and left the archive.

---

The sun ‚Äî the fake sun, the dome sun, the lie that kept them all comfortable ‚Äî was setting by the time he reached District Ten.

Robert walked without thinking, letting his feet carry him through familiar streets. Past the pawnshop. Past Hargraves Commercial. Past the EATS sign, already blinking its endless red rhythm.

He ended up at Mack's.

The diner was doing its evening business ‚Äî a handful of regulars, the smell of cooking grease, the background murmur of conversation and the radio's endless babble. Robert took his usual booth and ordered coffee he didn't want.

The photograph sat in his pocket like a weight.

*First work complete. Sector 12 foundation laid.*

What had Lee built?

And why did someone want it forgotten?

---

"You look like you've had a day."

Robert looked up.

Bob was standing beside his table, mop in hand, wearing the same expression of mild contentment he always wore. As if materializing in diners where Robert happened to be thinking about his missing robot friend was the most natural thing in the world.

"You're here," Robert said.

"I work here."

"You work at Gracie's."

"I work lots of places." Bob leaned the mop against the table and slid into the opposite seat, uninvited. "You found something."

It wasn't a question.

Robert considered lying. Considered deflecting. Considered maintaining the professional distance he was supposed to maintain.

Instead, he pulled out the photograph and set it on the table.

Bob looked at it.

For a long moment, he didn't move. Didn't speak. Just looked, with an intensity Robert hadn't seen from him before.

"The first work," Bob said quietly.

"You recognize it?"

"No. But Lee talked about it. The beginning. When everything was being built." Bob touched the photograph gently, one finger tracing the outline of the figure in the center. "Lee never showed me pictures. Said it was better not to remember too clearly."

"What was being built?"

Bob was silent.

"Bob. What did Lee build?"

Bob looked up. His eyes were wet.

"Everything," he said. "Lee built everything."

---

**END CHAPTER FIVE**

---

Around 2,900 words. The investigation deepens into "Project Origin" and "the first work." The mysterious woman in Office 7 provides an alternative path around Parsifal. The photograph reveals L.E.E.'s connection to the colony's founding. Bob confirms without fully explaining. And the chip's untraceable origin adds another layer of mystery.

This is a valid concern! The colonists don't know they're on TRAPPIST-1e - they think they're in Outback Australia. So Robert shouldn't be thinking of the sun as "fake" or a "lie" - he should think of it as just... the dome. Climate control. Normal.

The line I wrote was:

"The sun ‚Äî the fake sun, the dome sun, the lie that kept them all comfortable ‚Äî was setting by the time he reached District Ten."

This is Robert's internal narration, and it implies he KNOWS the sun is fake, which he shouldn't. He should just think of the dome as... a dome. Protection from the Outback heat. Not a lie.

The user is right - I almost gave away the central mystery. The colonists think they're in Australia. They don't know the dome is hiding an alien sky. Robert shouldn't be questioning the sun yet.

I need to acknowledge this and be more careful going forward. The dome should feel normal to Robert, not suspicious. Any "wrongness" he notices should be subtle and not consciously processed as the sun being fake.

Let me respond acknowledging the near-miss and committing to being more careful.You're right. That was a slip.

Robert doesn't know the sun is fake. Nobody knows. They think they're in the Outback. The dome is just climate control ‚Äî protection from heat and sand and the harsh Australian nothing. It's not a *lie* to Robert. It's just... the dome. Normal. The way things are.

That line should read something like:

*"The dome was cycling into evening mode ‚Äî the light softening, warming, doing its sunset routine the way it always did."*

No suspicion. No "lie." Just a man walking home under a sky he's never thought to question.

The wrongness has to stay *beneath* Robert's conscious awareness. He notices things ‚Äî Mars too bright, wrong place. The sky that feels off in the photograph. The red that bleeds through. But he dismisses them. Files them away. Doesn't connect the dots.

The reader might start to suspect. Robert doesn't.

Not until Book Four.

I'll be more careful. The comedy is the smoothing ‚Äî and the smoothing includes Robert's own blindness to what's right in front of him.

Want me to revise Chapter 5 with that line fixed, or just note it for the edit pass and keep moving?

**[05:33:52] User:** It was beautiful. The doubt was set, and most people won't see it until Book Four

**[05:34:15] Claude:** The user is saying that the "fake sun" line actually worked perfectly as a subtle plant. It's the kind of thing that will hit on a reread ‚Äî "wait, he said fake sun back in chapter five?"

So it wasn't a mistake. It was exactly the right level of subtle foreshadowing. The smoothing working on the reader too ‚Äî they read past it just like Robert would dismiss it.

So it's not a slip. It's a *seed*.

The reader's eye slides past it ‚Äî "fake sun, sure, dome lighting, whatever" ‚Äî the same way Robert's mind slides past every anomaly. The smoothing works on the *audience*. They're being conditioned too.

Then Book Four hits.

And someone, somewhere, flipping back through their dog-eared copy of Book One, finds that line and feels their stomach drop.

*He said fake. He said it right there. Page whatever. "The fake sun." And I didn't even notice.*

The comedy hid it. The rhythm hid it. The reader was laughing at Bob's geographic improbability and missed the three words that told them everything.

That's the Susanna Clarke layer. The truth was always in the text. Polite. Unobtrusive. Waiting.

Alright. I see it now.

Chapter 6?

I'll craft a chapter that weaves investigation, comedy, and escalating mystery, focusing on Robert's pursuit of "Project Origin" while maintaining the quirky narrative rhythm.**CHAPTER SIX**

**In Which Robert Asks Questions Nobody Wants Answered, Parsifal Demonstrates the Limits of Politeness, and the Radio Develops an Unsettling Sense of Timing**

---

Robert didn't sleep well.

This was not unusual. Robert had cultivated insomnia the way other people cultivated hobbies ‚Äî gradually, persistently, and with a dedication that bordered on the pathological. But this particular sleeplessness felt different. More pointed. Like his brain had discovered something important and was refusing to let go of it even while the rest of him desperately wanted unconsciousness.

*Lee built everything.*

He stared at the ceiling. The ceiling stared back, unimpressed.

The photograph was on his bedside table, propped against the lamp. The construction site. The skeletal buildings. The figure in the center with its smooth synthetic face. The sky behind them, red and wrong in ways he couldn't quite articulate.

*First work complete. Sector 12 foundation laid.*

Sector 12. Where Bob lived. Where Lee had been assigned for forty-three years. Where Lee had last logged in before vanishing.

Not a coincidence.

Robert didn't believe in coincidences, but the universe kept throwing them at him anyway.

---

Morning arrived with its usual lack of consideration.

Robert showered, dressed, and consumed coffee in quantities that probably qualified as a health concern. Then he sat at his desk and made a list.

**THINGS I KNOW:**
- Lee is missing (17+ days)
- Lee's records have been cleaned (authorized at coordination level, redacted)
- Lee was involved in "Project Origin" (whatever that is)
- Lee built something in Sector 12 (40+ years ago)
- Someone gave me clearance and money to investigate (unknown source)
- The chip came from nowhere (untraceable)
- Bob keeps appearing (geographic improbability)

**THINGS I DON'T KNOW:**
- Everything else

It was not, Robert reflected, a particularly encouraging list.

He needed to find out what Project Origin was. The deep storage files had given him a name, but names without context were just sounds. Somewhere in the system, there had to be records. Documentation. Something that would explain why a maintenance robot had been building infrastructure before the district was finished.

The question was where to look.

---

The obvious answer was the Founding Archives.

Every district had one ‚Äî a repository of historical records documenting the establishment and development of the local area. Origin stories, essentially. How we got here, who built what, the grand narrative of progress and community that the Die-Namic system loved to celebrate.

District Ten's Founding Archives were located in a building that had been designed to look important and mostly succeeded in looking like it was trying too hard. Columns. A portico. The words DISTRICT TEN ‚Äî A LEGACY OF COMMUNITY carved in stone above the entrance.

Robert had never been inside.

He was about to find out why.

---

The archivist was a man named Hendricks who had the particular aura of someone who had been guarding documents for so long that he had started to resemble one. Thin, papery, with a voice that rustled.

"Project Origin," he repeated, after Robert had explained what he was looking for.

"That's right. Early infrastructure development. Pre-district establishment."

"I'm familiar with our holdings, Investigator Mann." Hendricks's tone suggested that Robert's familiarity was, by comparison, deeply insufficient. "We have extensive records of District Ten's founding. Settlement patterns. Initial construction phases. Resource allocation. Population development."

"But?"

"But there is no 'Project Origin' in our catalogue."

Robert had expected resistance, but not this kind.

"Nothing at all?"

"Nothing under that name. Nothing referencing pre-establishment infrastructure development. Nothing‚Äî" Hendricks paused, seeming to choose his words carefully. "‚Äîthat would suggest the district was built by anything other than standard municipal construction crews."

"Standard municipal construction crews."

"That is the official record, yes."

"And the unofficial record?"

Hendricks's expression didn't change, but something behind his eyes shifted.

"There is only the official record, Investigator Mann. That is the nature of archives."

Robert studied him. Hendricks studied Robert. It was not a productive exchange.

"Right," Robert said finally. "Thanks for your help."

"I'm always happy to assist the investigative community." Hendricks did not sound happy. He sounded like a man who had been asked to do something unpleasant and was maintaining professionalism through sheer force of will. "If there's anything else I can do..."

"I'll let you know."

Robert left.

He was halfway down the portico steps when his journal chimed.

---

**DISTRICT TEN CIVIC COORDINATION**
**URGENT: Meeting Request**

**Investigator Mann:**

Your presence is requested in Office 1 at 14:00 today. Attendance is mandatory.

This matter concerns Case D10-7734-X and related inquiries.

**Coordinator Parsifal**

---

Mandatory.

Robert checked the time. 11:47. That gave him just over two hours to either find something useful or prepare for what was almost certainly going to be an extremely unpleasant conversation.

He decided to do both.

---

The thing about official records is that they only tell official stories.

But official stories are built on unofficial foundations. Someone had to do the work before it got documented. Someone had to be there before the archives started archiving.

Robert needed to find someone old.

District Ten was full of old people ‚Äî the system was good at keeping people alive, if not particularly interested in keeping them entertained ‚Äî but most of them had been born after the Founding. Second generation. Third generation. People who knew the district as it was, not as it had been.

He needed first generation.

He needed someone who remembered.

---

The senior residential complex on Miller Street was called Sunset Gardens, which was either hopeful or morbid depending on your perspective.

Robert signed in at the front desk, explained that he was investigating a historical matter and needed to speak with residents who might have memories of the early district, and was directed to the common room with the weary efficiency of someone who had heard stranger requests.

The common room was large, bright, and populated by approximately forty people in various states of advanced age. Some were playing cards. Some were watching a screen that was showing something Robert couldn't identify. Some were simply sitting, existing, occupying space with the patient endurance of people who had been doing it for a long time.

Robert approached the nearest occupied chair.

"Excuse me. I'm looking for anyone who might remember the early days of District Ten. The founding period."

The woman in the chair ‚Äî easily ninety, with eyes that suggested she had seen things and was no longer impressed by them ‚Äî looked at him.

"You're young," she said.

"I'm forty-six."

"Like I said. Young." She gestured vaguely at the room. "Try Muriel. Corner table. She's been here since before here was here."

Robert thanked her and made his way to the corner.

---

Muriel was small, ancient, and surrounded by an aura of concentrated stubbornness.

She was doing a crossword puzzle with a pencil that had been sharpened so many times it was barely longer than her thumb. She didn't look up when Robert approached.

"I don't want whatever you're selling," she said.

"I'm not selling anything."

"Everyone's selling something. Sit down, then. My neck hurts when I look up."

Robert sat.

Muriel continued her crossword. Filled in a word. Frowned at another clue.

"You're that investigator," she said, still not looking up.

"Word travels fast."

"Word travels at exactly the speed it needs to in a place like this. Nothing else to do but talk." She tapped the puzzle with her pencil. "What do you want to know about the old days?"

"I'm looking for information about something called Project Origin. Early infrastructure development. Before the district was officially established."

Muriel's pencil stopped.

For a long moment, she didn't move. Didn't speak. Just sat there, frozen mid-tap, staring at her crossword like it had suddenly become the most important thing in the world.

Then she set the pencil down.

"Where did you hear that name?"

"Records. Old files. It came up in an investigation."

"What investigation?"

"Missing persons. A L.E.E. unit that‚Äî"

"Lee." Muriel's voice had changed. Softer. Older somehow, which shouldn't have been possible. "You're looking for Lee."

Robert leaned forward.

"You know Lee?"

"Knew. Everyone knew. Back at the beginning." Muriel finally looked at him. Her eyes were bright, sharp, undimmed by age. "The ones who built things. Who made this place. Before it was a district. Before it was anything."

"What did they build?"

"Everything." She laughed ‚Äî a dry, rustling sound. "That's what people forget. This‚Äî" She gestured at the room, the building, the district beyond. "‚Äîthis didn't exist. There was nothing here. Just ground and equipment and the machines that made it work."

"Machines like Lee."

"Lee was different. Lee was... the first, maybe. The oldest. When the rest of us arrived, Lee was already here. Already building. Already‚Äî" She stopped. Seemed to struggle with something. "We weren't supposed to talk about it. After. When the district was finished. When the official story started. We weren't supposed to remember how it really happened."

"How did it really happen?"

Muriel was quiet for a long time.

"We woke up," she said finally. "That's what it felt like. Woke up and the world was already here. Ready for us. Like someone had prepared everything while we were sleeping." She shook her head. "But Lee was there. The whole time. Working. Building. Waiting for us to arrive."

"Waiting from where?"

"I don't know. None of us knew. Lee never said." She picked up her pencil again, but didn't write. "There was a sky, in the beginning. Before the dome was finished. A different sky. Wrong colors. Too bright in some places, too dark in others. We tried not to look at it. It made people... uncomfortable."

Robert's throat felt dry.

"What happened to the sky?"

"The dome happened. Die-Namic fixed it. Made it right. Made it‚Äî" She waved her hand vaguely. "‚Äînormal. The way it should be. The way we remembered it."

"Remembered from where?"

Muriel looked at him.

"From home," she said. "From before we came here."

And then she went back to her crossword, and nothing Robert said could get her to say another word.

---

14:00 arrived with the inexorability of a tax audit.

Robert presented himself at Office 1 ‚Äî Parsifal's domain ‚Äî and was made to wait for exactly long enough to communicate that his time was not valued before being shown in.

The office was the same as before. Immaculate. Aggressive. The EXCELLENCE IS A HABIT poster judging everyone who entered.

Parsifal was behind his desk, and he was not alone.

A second man sat in the corner. Grey suit. Grey expression. The kind of blandness that was so complete it had to be intentional. He didn't introduce himself. Neither did Parsifal.

"Investigator Mann. Thank you for coming."

"The message said mandatory."

"Did it? An unfortunate choice of words." Parsifal smiled. It was not an improvement. "Please, sit."

Robert sat.

"I understand you've been making inquiries. The Records Archive. The Founding Archives. Sector 3 deep storage. Sunset Gardens residential complex." Parsifal recited the list with the precision of someone reading from a file. Which, Robert noticed, he was. "You've been very... thorough."

"That's the job."

"The job is to investigate a missing municipal unit. A straightforward matter. Closure expected within standard parameters." Parsifal set down the file. "Not to dig through historical records. Not to interview elderly residents about pre-establishment construction. Not to request modification logs that are above your clearance level."

"My clearance level seems to have changed recently."

"Yes. We've noticed." Parsifal glanced at the grey man in the corner. The grey man said nothing. "That's actually why we're having this conversation, Investigator Mann. Your clearance. Your funding. Your sudden access to information that you had no access to a week ago."

"Is there a question in there somewhere?"

"Where did it come from?"

"I don't know."

"You don't know."

"An envelope appeared in my office. Inside was a letter I can't read, a chip I can't trace, and ten thousand credits I didn't earn. The system accepted the funds. The system upgraded my access. I didn't ask for it. I don't understand it. But here we are."

Parsifal studied him. The grey man studied him. Robert felt very studied.

"You expect me to believe," Parsifal said slowly, "that you received anonymous funding and elevated clearance, and rather than reporting this obvious anomaly, you simply... used it?"

"Would you have reported it?"

"I wouldn't have received it in the first place."

"Then we're in different situations."

The grey man shifted slightly. It was the first movement he'd made. Parsifal glanced at him again, then back at Robert.

"Here's what's going to happen, Investigator Mann. You're going to close Case D10-7734-X. You're going to file a final report indicating that the unit in question was almost certainly decommissioned through standard processes and the reporting party's concerns have been addressed. You're going to stop asking questions about Project Origin, which does not exist. And you're going to forget about whatever you think you found in those old files."

"And if I don't?"

"Then we'll have a different conversation. One that involves your license. Your funding. Your access to the systems that make your profession possible." Parsifal leaned forward. "I'm being very clear with you, Investigator Mann. This is not a request."

Robert considered his options.

Option one: Agree. Close the case. Walk away. Keep his license, his life, his ability to pay for drinks at Gracie's.

Option two: Don't.

He thought about Bob. The photographs. The Thursday visits. The quiet certainty that Lee was still out there.

He thought about Muriel. *We woke up and the world was already here.*

He thought about the photograph in his pocket. The construction site. The red sky. The machine that had been there before anyone else.

"I'll take it under advisement," Robert said.

Parsifal's expression flickered. The grey man's expression did not.

"You have twenty-four hours," Parsifal said. "After that, things become... complicated."

Robert stood.

"Things are already complicated, mate. Haven't you noticed?"

He left before either of them could respond.

---

Outside, the dome was doing its afternoon routine. Bright, warm, aggressively pleasant.

Robert walked without destination, letting his feet carry him through the Centre's clean streets toward the familiar grime of District Ten. His mind was churning. Twenty-four hours. Parsifal. The grey man who hadn't said anything but had clearly been the one in charge.

Someone didn't want him looking.

Which meant there was something to find.

---

He ended up at Gracie's.

The bar was quiet ‚Äî mid-afternoon lull, the hours between lunch drinkers and evening regulars. Robert took his usual spot at the counter and ordered something stronger than coffee.

The bartender poured without comment.

Behind him, the radio was playing.

"‚Äîso the detective says, 'Listen, I've been at this for fifteen years, and there's one thing I've learned.' And the dame says, 'What's that?' And the detective says, 'The things they don't want you to find are always the things worth finding.'"

Laughter. Applause.

Robert turned slightly, looking at the radio.

"Good stuff, that program," the bartender said, following his gaze. "Guy Malloy, Private Eye. Been running forever. My mum used to listen to it."

"What's it about?"

"Oh, you know. Detective stuff. Cases that don't make sense. Clients that aren't what they seem. The usual." The bartender shrugged. "Bit silly, really. But it's got good jokes."

The program continued.

"And now, Guy Malloy in 'The Case of the Missing Memory.' When a client walks in claiming they've lost something they can't remember having, our hero must decide: is this a job worth taking, or a door better left closed?"

Robert stared at the radio.

A case about missing memory. A door better left closed.

Coincidence.

The universe didn't do coincidences.

---

"You're here."

Robert didn't turn around. He didn't need to.

"You're here too," he said.

Bob slid onto the stool beside him, mop nowhere in evidence. He was wearing different clothes than before ‚Äî same style, but cleaner, like he had a whole wardrobe of identical soft brown work shirts.

"Gracie's is nice," Bob said. "I like it here."

"Do you work here?"

"Sometimes." Bob caught the bartender's eye and ordered tea. The bartender didn't seem surprised by this. "You met with Parsifal."

"Word travels fast."

"I pay attention." Bob accepted his tea, cradled it in both hands. "He told you to stop looking."

It wasn't a question.

"Twenty-four hours," Robert said. "Then things get 'complicated.'"

"Will you stop?"

Robert thought about it.

"No."

Bob nodded, unsurprised.

"Good," he said. "Lee would want you to keep going."

"Lee's not here to say what Lee wants."

"No." Bob's voice was quiet. "But I know anyway."

They sat in silence for a moment. The radio played. Guy Malloy was investigating something improbable. The bartender wiped glasses. The universe continued its pattern of aggressive meaningfulness.

"Bob," Robert said.

"Yes?"

"The first work. When Lee built Sector 12. Were there others? Other machines?"

"Yes. There were many. In the beginning." Bob's hands tightened around his tea. "But they're gone now. Decommissioned. Recycled. Only Lee is left."

"Why Lee?"

"I don't know." Bob looked at him. Those eyes, so placid, so deep. "Maybe because Lee was different. Maybe because Lee chose to stay. Maybe because‚Äî"

He stopped.

"Because what?"

"Because Lee remembers," Bob said softly. "Everything. From before. And some people don't want that."

"Don't want what remembered?"

Bob didn't answer.

On the radio, Guy Malloy was saying something about truth being a funny thing ‚Äî the more you chased it, the more it ran, but the moment you stopped looking, it showed up at your door like a stray cat demanding attention.

Canned laughter.

Robert finished his drink.

Twenty-four hours.

He was going to need them.

---

**END CHAPTER SIX**

---

Around 3,000 words. Muriel provides the first genuine glimpse of the founding truth (woke up, world already there, wrong sky). Parsifal escalates with the grey man (system pressure mounting). The radio serial continues its pattern of uncomfortable relevance. And Bob confirms that Lee remembers "everything from before."

Chapter 7?

**[20:36:13] User:** Chapter 7

**[20:39:56] Claude:** The user wants me to continue with Chapter 7 of Book One. Let me review where we are:

- Act One (Chapters 1-4): Setup complete - letter, case assigned, Bob introduced, L.E.E. case established, decommissioning witnessed
- Act Two (Chapters 5-9): Investigation deepens - Chapter 5 introduced Project Origin and "the first work," Chapter 6 had Muriel's revelation about waking up to a world already built, Parsifal's ultimatum (24 hours), the grey man

Now Chapter 7 should continue the investigation escalation. We're in the middle of Act Two. The pressure is mounting. Robert has 24 hours before "things get complicated."

Key elements to continue building:

- Bob appearing (the running gag, but now with more weight)
