Let me write Chapter 7 with the sitcom pacing, Guy Noir voice, and the layered comedy that hides the deeper architecture.**CHAPTER SEVEN**

**In Which Robert Makes Questionable Decisions on a Deadline, Discovers That Some Doors Lock Behind You, and the Janitor Demonstrates an Unexpected Skill Set**

---

Twenty-four hours is not a lot of time.

It's not nothing ‚Äî you can do quite a bit in twenty-four hours if you're motivated, caffeinated, and unburdened by things like common sense or self-preservation. But it's also not the kind of timeline that encourages careful planning and methodical investigation.

Robert was going to have to be neither careful nor methodical.

This was, historically speaking, his specialty.

---

The night after Parsifal's ultimatum, Robert didn't go home.

He went to the maintenance corridors.

The access point in Sector 12 opened for him the same way it had before ‚Äî palm scan, slight pause, green light, unlocked door. His elevated clearance was still working. Either nobody had thought to revoke it yet, or someone wanted him to keep using it.

Robert wasn't sure which option was more concerning.

The corridors were different at night. Quieter. The blue light strips seemed dimmer, more uncertain. The hum of machinery that had been background noise during the day became something closer to a presence ‚Äî a low, constant vibration that Robert could feel in his back teeth.

He made his way to Junction 12-E-7.

The room was the same as before. Screens, pipes, the single chair facing the dark control interface. But this time, Robert wasn't looking at the logs. He was looking for something else.

*Unit demonstrates unexpected capacity for independent decision-making.*

That's what the memo had said. The one in the deep storage files. Independent decision-making. Which meant Lee had done something, forty years ago, that wasn't in the programming. Something that had prompted observation. Documentation.

Documentation that had been almost entirely erased.

But not quite.

Robert started opening access panels.

---

The thing about old infrastructure is that it accumulates.

New systems get layered on top of old systems. Updates get applied without removing what came before. Forty years of maintenance meant forty years of patches, modifications, workarounds, and the particular kind of technical debt that happened when nobody wanted to do a full rebuild because the current setup worked well enough.

Lee had worked in this junction for four decades.

Lee had left marks.

Robert found the first one behind a panel near the floor ‚Äî a series of scratches in the metal, too regular to be accidental. Lines and curves, grouped in clusters. Not words. Not numbers. Something else.

He photographed it with his journal.

The second mark was on the underside of the control interface ‚Äî more scratches, same pattern, but arranged differently. Like a variation on a theme.

The third was behind the chair itself, carved into the wall at about knee height if you were sitting. This one was larger, more detailed. The same basic elements, but combined into something that almost looked like a diagram.

Robert stared at it.

Seventeen clusters. Arranged in a specific pattern. Connected by lines that suggested relationship, sequence, order.

*Seventeen.*

Bob's kata. The one he'd tapped before he could speak. Before Lee taught him to sign.

These weren't random marks. They were a language. Or the foundation of one. Something Lee had developed, refined, used to communicate with a child who couldn't communicate any other way.

And Lee had carved it into the infrastructure.

Into the walls.

Into the bones of the district.

---

Robert was so focused on the markings that he almost didn't hear the footsteps.

Almost.

The sound was soft ‚Äî the particular careful tread of someone trying not to be heard ‚Äî but the corridors were quiet enough that it carried. Robert froze, journal still in hand, suddenly very aware that he was alone in a restricted area at two in the morning with no good explanation for his presence.

The footsteps stopped.

Robert held his breath.

A long moment passed. Then another.

Then the lights went out.

---

Darkness in a maintenance corridor is not like darkness anywhere else.

There are no windows. No ambient glow from the dome. No streetlights, no signage, no helpful bioluminescent fungi. Just nothing ‚Äî a complete absence of light that pressed against Robert's eyes like a physical thing.

He didn't move.

Didn't breathe.

The footsteps resumed. Closer now. Coming from the direction of the main corridor.

Robert's hand found his journal. The left side ‚Äî his toolkit. Low-volt weapon. Not much good against anything serious, but better than nothing. He thumbed it active, felt the slight vibration that indicated it was charged and ready.

The footsteps stopped again.

Right outside the junction door.

Robert could hear breathing. Not his own ‚Äî he was still holding his breath, lungs burning ‚Äî but someone else's. Slow. Measured. Patient.

Then a voice.

"You should not be here, Investigator Mann."

Robert didn't recognize it. Low, flat, without inflection. The kind of voice that had been trained to sound like nothing at all.

"Door was open," Robert managed.

"The door will not be open much longer. Neither will your investigation." A pause. "You were warned. You did not listen. This is... disappointing."

"Story of my life."

"Your life is not the story that concerns us." The voice was closer now. Just outside the door. "The unit you are seeking does not exist. The records you are chasing do not exist. The history you are uncovering does not exist. There is only the official narrative. There has only ever been the official narrative."

"Then why are you here?"

Silence.

Robert's eyes were starting to adjust ‚Äî not to light, because there wasn't any, but to the particular texture of darkness. He could make out the vague shape of the doorway. The slightly-less-dark rectangle against the dark.

And in that rectangle, a figure. Standing. Waiting.

"Leave this alone, Investigator Mann. Close your case. Return to your life. This is not a warning. This is a gift."

"Funny way to wrap a gift. Usually they come with bows, not threats in dark corridors."

"You misunderstand. The gift is that you are still receiving warnings." The figure shifted slightly. "Others have not been so fortunate."

Robert's grip tightened on his weapon.

"Others like Lee?"

The silence that followed was different. Heavier. Like the darkness itself had thickened.

"Lee," the voice said finally, "made choices. Choices have consequences. Even for machines."

"So Lee is still functional."

"I did not say that."

"You didn't not say it either."

Another pause. Then, impossibly, a sound that might have been a laugh. Dry, brief, utterly without humor.

"You are persistent, Investigator Mann. This is not a compliment." The figure stepped back from the doorway. "Your clearance will be revoked within the hour. Your access will be terminated. Your case will be closed, by you or by others. The only question is whether you will still be practicing your profession when it happens."

Footsteps. Receding.

Robert waited until he couldn't hear them anymore. Then he waited some more, because paranoia had kept him alive this long and he wasn't about to stop trusting it now.

Then the lights came back on.

---

The corridor was empty.

No sign of anyone. No footprints in the dust ‚Äî was there dust? There was now, apparently, and it was undisturbed. No evidence that anyone had been there at all except Robert and his racing heart.

He checked his journal.

The photographs of the markings were still there. The time showed 2:47 AM. His clearance status showed... unchanged. Still elevated. Still active.

*Within the hour*, the voice had said.

Robert had fifty-three minutes. Maybe less.

He started running.

---

Junction 12-E-7 was connected to the main coordination hub by approximately half a kilometer of maintenance corridor, which was considerably longer when you were running through it at top speed while expecting your access to be cut at any moment.

Robert made it in four minutes. His lungs were not happy about this. His legs were filing formal complaints. His heart was threatening to unionize.

The hub was a larger space ‚Äî a central node where multiple corridor branches converged, filled with monitoring equipment and coordination terminals. During the day, technicians would be here, managing the district's environmental systems. At three in the morning, it was empty.

Robert went straight for the primary terminal.

His clearance still worked. He had maybe forty-five minutes.

He pulled up the coordination logs ‚Äî not the public ones, not the accessible ones, but the deep system records that tracked everything that happened in the district's infrastructure. Every access point. Every maintenance event. Every anomaly.

He searched for Lee.

The results came back almost instantly. Unit 7734. Activity log. Forty-three years of recorded actions, compressed into a scrolling wall of data that would take days to read properly.

Robert didn't have days. He had forty minutes.

He narrowed the search. Last known location. Last logged activity. Anything unusual in the final weeks before disappearance.

The system processed.

And then it showed him something strange.

---

Lee's final logged activity was at Junction 12-E-7. That much Robert already knew. Standard diagnostic cycle, fifteen minutes, logout at 14:47 on the day of disappearance.

But there was another entry. Flagged as an anomaly. Timestamped twelve minutes *after* the logout.

*14:59 ‚Äî Unit 7734 ‚Äî Access Point 12-E-SUBSURFACE ‚Äî Entry Logged*

Subsurface.

Robert stared at the entry.

The district's infrastructure had multiple levels ‚Äî everyone knew that. Surface level for daily operations, mid-level for utilities, deep level for foundations and emergency systems. But subsurface? That wasn't a designation Robert had ever seen.

He searched for Access Point 12-E-SUBSURFACE.

*No results found.*

He searched for any subsurface access points in Sector 12.

*No results found.*

He searched for subsurface as a general term.

*Access denied. Clearance insufficient.*

His elevated clearance. The clearance that had opened every door for the past week. Insufficient.

Which meant there were levels above his level.

Which meant there were things even ten thousand credits couldn't buy.

---

Robert checked the time. Thirty-one minutes.

He copied what he could ‚Äî the activity logs, the anomaly flag, the coordinates embedded in the subsurface entry. Then he cleared his search history, logged out, and headed for the exit.

He was halfway to the access point when his journal chimed.

---

**SYSTEM NOTIFICATION**
**Clearance Status Update**

**Investigator R. P. Mann ‚Äî License #D10-4471**

Your temporary clearance elevation has been revoked effective immediately. Access to restricted areas is no longer authorized. Any current presence in restricted areas should be terminated immediately.

Thank you for your cooperation.

**This is an automated message. Do not reply.**

---

Robert looked at the access door ahead of him.

The light was red.

He looked at the access door behind him.

Also red.

He was locked in.

---

For a long moment, Robert just stood there, contemplating the particular stupidity that had led him to this point.

He could try to override the locks ‚Äî his toolkit had some basic bypass capabilities ‚Äî but that would trigger alarms, summon security, and turn a bad situation into a catastrophic one. He could wait until morning, when the maintenance crews arrived, but that would involve explaining why a licensed investigator was trapped in a restricted corridor at three in the morning, and the explanation would not go well.

He could‚Äî

The access door behind him clicked.

Robert spun, weapon raised.

The door opened.

Bob stepped through.

---

"Hello," Bob said.

Robert stared at him.

"You're here."

"Yes."

"In a restricted maintenance corridor. At three in the morning. That was locked until you opened it."

"Yes." Bob looked at the door like it had done something mildly interesting. "I know the patterns."

"The patterns."

"The locks. The codes. Lee taught me." Bob shrugged ‚Äî a simple movement that somehow communicated vast depths of unconcern. "Lee taught me lots of things. In case I needed them."

"In case you needed to break into restricted areas?"

"In case I needed to help." Bob looked at him. That direct gaze. Those eyes that saw too much. "You need help."

Robert's weapon was still raised. He lowered it. Slowly.

"How did you know I was here?"

"I pay attention."

"You pay attention to my location at three in the morning?"

"I pay attention to everything." Bob turned back toward the door. "We should go. They'll send people soon."

"Who's 'they'?"

"The ones who don't want you to find things." Bob stepped through the doorway, into the corridor beyond. "Coming?"

Robert followed.

He didn't have a better option.

---

They moved through the maintenance corridors in silence.

Bob navigated with the casual certainty of someone who had been walking these paths for years ‚Äî which, Robert realized, he probably had. Lee's territory. Lee's routes. Learned and memorized and carried by the student when the teacher disappeared.

Every locked door they encountered opened at Bob's touch.

"How?" Robert asked, after the third one.

"Lee's patterns." Bob's hands moved as he walked ‚Äî small, repetitive gestures that Robert now recognized as some kind of signing. "The locks respond to the patterns. Lee built them that way."

"Lee built the locks?"

"Lee built lots of things." Bob stopped at an intersection, considered, turned left. "The district thinks it built itself. Thinks it grew naturally. Like a plant. But plants have seeds. Someone has to plant the seeds."

"Lee planted the seeds."

"Lee planted everything."

They emerged from the corridors through an access point Robert had never seen ‚Äî a small door in a wall that looked like solid brick until Bob touched it in exactly the right way. Beyond was an alley, dark and narrow, somewhere in the industrial section of Sector 12.

Robert breathed actual air for the first time in what felt like hours.

"Bob."

"Yes?"

"I found something. In the junction. Lee's last recorded location." Robert pulled up the copied data on his journal. "There's a reference to something called 'subsurface.' An access point. Lee went there, after the official logout. Twelve minutes after."

Bob looked at the data. His expression didn't change, but something in his stillness shifted.

"Subsurface," he repeated.

"You know what it is?"

"No." Bob's voice was quiet. "Lee never mentioned it. Lee never‚Äî" He stopped. His hands, which had been moving constantly, went still. "Lee kept secrets. Even from me. Said it was safer that way. Said I would understand someday."

"Do you? Understand?"

Bob looked at him.

"Not yet," he said. "But I think you're going to help me."

---

They parted at the edge of Sector 12.

Bob disappeared into the pre-dawn darkness with the same impossible stealth he'd demonstrated in the corridors. One moment he was there; the next, he wasn't. Like a magic trick. Like a ghost.

Like someone who had learned to be invisible from something very old and very good at not being seen.

Robert walked home.

The dome was starting its sunrise routine ‚Äî that slow brightening that was supposed to feel natural and instead felt like a theatrical production. Artificial dawn for artificial people in an artificial world.

He thought about the voice in the darkness. *The unit you are seeking does not exist.*

He thought about Muriel. *We woke up and the world was already here.*

He thought about the subsurface access point that didn't appear in any database he could reach.

Lee had gone somewhere. Twelve minutes after officially logging out. To a place that wasn't supposed to exist.

And someone very badly didn't want Robert to find out where.

---

His flat was cold.

Robert turned on the heat, collapsed into his chair, and stared at the ceiling until the sun finished its fake rising and the district came to life around him.

His journal showed seventeen missed notifications. All from Civic Coordination. All marked URGENT.

He ignored them.

On the desk, the letter sat where he'd left it. T. A. F. Three letters that still meant nothing. A mystery inside a mystery inside a case that he'd been ordered to close and couldn't.

*Tag along, fella.*

Bob, appearing everywhere. Bob, knowing the patterns. Bob, opening doors that shouldn't open.

*Taos Articulating Frequency.*

The words surfaced from somewhere ‚Äî a phrase he'd seen, maybe, in one of the old files. One of the redacted documents. He couldn't remember where.

He pulled up his journal and searched his notes.

Nothing.

He searched the copied coordination logs.

Nothing.

He searched his memory, that unreliable archive of half-formed impressions and forgotten details.

*Taos.*

Where had he seen that word?

The radio.

The stupid detective program that kept playing in every bar and diner he walked into. Guy Malloy, Private Eye. There had been an episode ‚Äî or a segment ‚Äî something about a frequency. A signal. A pattern that connected things that shouldn't be connected.

He couldn't remember the details.

He couldn't remember if he'd actually heard it or just imagined he had.

The dome was fully bright now. Morning in District Ten. Another day in a life that had become significantly more complicated than he'd expected.

Robert's journal chimed again. Another urgent notification.

He looked at it.

---

**DISTRICT TEN CIVIC COORDINATION**
**FINAL NOTICE: Case D10-7734-X**

**Investigator Mann:**

Your investigation has been officially closed by administrative action. No further inquiries are authorized. Any continuation of investigation-related activities will be considered a violation of your licensing agreement and will result in immediate revocation and potential criminal referral.

Your cooperation is appreciated.

**Coordinator Parsifal**

---

Case closed.

Investigation terminated.

Robert was officially done.

He read the notification twice. Then he archived it. Then he opened a new file.

*Personal Notes ‚Äî Unofficial ‚Äî Not Case Related*

He started typing.

---

**END CHAPTER SEVEN**

---

Around 2,900 words. The stakes escalate dramatically ‚Äî Robert is directly threatened, trapped, and rescued by Bob demonstrating skills Lee taught him. The "subsurface" mystery is introduced as Lee's last known location. Bob reveals he doesn't know everything Lee knew. And Robert's official investigation is terminated, forcing him to go underground.

**In Which Robert Becomes Officially Unofficial, the Radio Achieves New Heights of Coincidence, and Someone Finally Explains What a Subsurface Actually Is**

---

There's a particular freedom that comes with having nothing left to lose.

Robert had lost his clearance. Lost his case. Lost, according to the seventeen increasingly aggressive notifications from Civic Coordination, approximately eighty percent of his professional standing and one hundred percent of Parsifal's patience.

What he hadn't lost was curiosity.

Curiosity, it turned out, didn't require a license.

---

The first thing Robert did, after officially becoming unofficial, was take a shower.

This was not a strategic decision. It was a biological necessity. He'd been awake for approximately thirty-one hours, had spent a significant portion of those hours in maintenance corridors being threatened by disembodied voices, and had reached the point where his own smell was becoming a distraction.

The shower helped.

The coffee helped more.

By nine in the morning, Robert had achieved something approximating human functionality and was ready to make a series of decisions that future Robert would almost certainly regret.

He started with the radio.

---

The thing about Guy Malloy, Private Eye was that it was everywhere.

Not literally ‚Äî it wasn't broadcast on every frequency, playing in every establishment, embedded in the district's acoustic wallpaper. But it felt that way. Every bar Robert walked into, every diner, every waiting room and transit station and corner shop, seemed to have the program playing at exactly the moment he arrived.

Coincidence.

Robert had stopped believing in coincidences approximately three days ago.

He searched for the program on his journal. Basic information only ‚Äî his access to detailed broadcasting records had been revoked along with everything else.

*Guy Malloy, Private Eye. Entertainment serial. Broadcast weekly. Produced by District Ten Community Broadcasting. Running time: 43 years.*

Forty-three years.

The same amount of time Lee had been operating in Sector 12.

Robert stared at the number.

It was probably nothing. Forty-three years was a long time. Lots of things had been running for forty-three years. The district itself had been fully established for about that long. It was a natural coincidence.

Except.

He searched for District Ten Community Broadcasting.

*District Ten Community Broadcasting. Established [DATE REDACTED]. Primary function: entertainment content production and distribution. Oversight: Die-Namic Cultural Affairs Division. Location: [RESTRICTED].*

Redacted. Restricted.

A community broadcasting service with a classified establishment date and a hidden location.

Robert added it to his mental list of things that didn't make sense.

The list was getting long.

---

His second decision was to visit the one person who might know what "subsurface" meant without setting off every alarm in the coordination system.

The woman from Office 7.

He didn't know her name. She hadn't offered it, and he hadn't asked. But she'd helped him before ‚Äî given him the address for the deep storage archive, pointed him toward answers when the official channels were busy being unhelpful. She'd said she was old and tired and had watched the system become something it wasn't supposed to be.

Maybe she was tired enough to help again.

---

Civic Coordination was, predictably, not thrilled to see him.

The front desk clerk ‚Äî a different one than before, younger, with the specific nervousness of someone who had been briefed about a problem and was now facing it ‚Äî looked at Robert's credentials with an expression of mild panic.

"Investigator Mann. You're... your access has been..."

"Revoked. Terminated. Administratively actioned. Yes, I'm aware." Robert smiled. It was not a reassuring smile. "I'm not here in an official capacity. I'm here to visit someone."

"Visit... someone?"

"Office 7. The woman who handles... whatever it is she handles."

The clerk's panic intensified.

"Office 7 is not... I don't have anyone listed for Office 7."

"She was there three days ago."

"I don't have any record of‚Äî" The clerk stopped. Checked something on the terminal. Checked it again. "There's no Office 7."

Robert blinked.

"I was there. End of the corridor, Section C. Unmarked door."

"There's no Office 7," the clerk repeated. The panic had been replaced by something else ‚Äî confusion, maybe, or the particular discomfort of someone whose reality had just been contradicted. "Section C ends at Office 6. It's always ended at Office 6."

Robert considered arguing. Considered insisting. Considered the possibility that he had imagined the entire encounter ‚Äî the cluttered office, the paper documents, the sharp-eyed woman who had known more than she should.

He didn't think he'd imagined it.

But someone had made sure it couldn't be verified.

"Right," Robert said. "My mistake."

He left before the clerk could call security.

---

The corridor in Section C ended at Office 6.

Robert stood in front of it, staring at the wall where Office 7 should have been. The wall was solid. Painted. Unremarkable. The kind of wall that had been there forever and would be there forever and had never contained a door to a cluttered office full of paper records and uncomfortable truths.

He touched it.

Solid.

He knocked.

Nothing.

He pressed his ear against it, feeling ridiculous, listening for any sound that might indicate a space beyond.

Silence.

"Can I help you?"

Robert turned.

A man was standing in the corridor ‚Äî middle-aged, bureaucratic, wearing the particular expression of someone who had found something unexpected and was deciding whether to report it.

"Just looking for someone," Robert said.

"In the wall?"

"It's a long story."

The man's expression shifted toward the decision to report.

"I'm leaving," Robert said. "Right now. Leaving."

He left.

---

The third decision was the questionable one.

If the official channels were closed ‚Äî and they were ‚Äî and the unofficial channels had apparently ceased to exist ‚Äî which they had ‚Äî then Robert needed to find someone outside the system entirely.

Someone who knew things they shouldn't know.

Someone who existed in the spaces between official and unofficial.

Someone who appeared in every diner, bar, and restricted maintenance corridor Robert happened to find himself in.

He needed to find Bob.

---

The problem with finding Bob was that Bob found you.

Robert had never actually sought Bob out. Bob had simply... appeared. At Gracie's. At Mack's. At the decommissioning facility. At three in the morning in a locked maintenance corridor. Always present, always unexpected, always exactly where Robert happened to need someone to be.

But Robert couldn't just wait for Bob to materialize. He had limited time, no clearance, and the distinct impression that people were watching him. If he wanted answers, he needed to ask questions, and if he wanted to ask questions, he needed to find the one person who seemed to have them.

He went to Bob's flat.

---

Harmony Gardens looked the same as before ‚Äî the optimistic name, the dying plant, the bench that had probably been sat on once and then forgotten. Robert found the right door and knocked.

Nothing.

He knocked again.

Still nothing.

He was considering his options ‚Äî wait, leave, break in and face the consequences ‚Äî when a voice behind him said, "He's not there."

Robert turned.

A woman was standing in the courtyard. Elderly, stooped, wearing a housecoat that had seen better decades. She was watching Robert with the particular attention of someone who had nothing better to do than observe her neighbors and their visitors.

"Bob," Robert said. "Robert Mann. The man who lives here."

"I know who Bob is." The woman's voice was sharp, unimpressed. "He's not there. Hasn't been for days."

"Do you know where he went?"

"Do I look like his secretary?"

"You look like someone who pays attention."

The woman considered this. Seemed to find it acceptable.

"He comes and goes," she said. "Always has. Strange hours. Strange habits. But he's been gone longer this time. Since..." She paused, calculating. "Since about a week ago. Maybe more."

A week ago. Around the time Lee disappeared.

"Did he say anything? Before he left?"

"Bob doesn't say much of anything. Not to me, anyway." The woman shuffled closer, curiosity overcoming caution. "You're that investigator. The one asking about the old robot."

"Word travels fast."

"Word is the only thing that travels in this place." She studied him. "You should be careful. Asking questions. Some questions have teeth."

"So I've been told."

"Have you been listening?"

Robert didn't have a good answer for that.

The woman shook her head ‚Äî the universal gesture of someone who had seen too many people ignore good advice.

"If you find Bob," she said, "tell him Mrs. Chen wants her cake pan back. He borrowed it six months ago and I'm starting to think he ate it."

"The cake or the pan?"

"With Bob? Could be either."

She shuffled away, leaving Robert alone in the courtyard with a missing person and a mystery he couldn't officially investigate.

---

He tried Gracie's.

Bob wasn't there.

He tried Mack's.

Bob wasn't there either.

He tried three other establishments where he'd seen Bob in the past week ‚Äî the noodle shop in the Margins, the transit station on Miller Street, the corner store that sold newspapers and optimism in equal measure.

No Bob.

The universe, which had been aggressively providing Bob at every opportunity, had apparently decided to stop.

Robert sat on a bench in a small plaza near the Sector 12 boundary and contemplated his options.

He had: no clearance, no case, no access, no Bob, and no idea what "subsurface" meant or how to find it.

He had: a letter he couldn't read, a chip that came from nowhere, photographs of scratches on walls, and the distinct impression that he was missing something obvious.

He had: approximately fourteen hours until his twenty-four-hour deadline expired and things presumably got worse.

The plaza was quiet. Mid-afternoon lull. A few people walking through, none of them paying attention to the man on the bench having an existential crisis about robots and restricted areas.

The plaza also had a public broadcast speaker, mounted on a pole near the fountain.

The speaker was playing Guy Malloy, Private Eye.

---

"‚Äîand so I told the dame, I said, 'Lady, I've been in this business long enough to know that when someone tells you not to look somewhere, that's exactly where you need to look.'"

Robert stared at the speaker.

"'But the door is locked,' she says. 'The records are sealed. The people who know aren't talking.' And I say, 'Then you find someone who doesn't know they know. Someone who was there but doesn't remember. Someone who built the thing but never saw the blueprints.'"

The speaker crackled. Music swelled. A voice announced: "Guy Malloy will return after these messages."

A jingle for nutritional supplements began playing.

Robert continued staring.

*Someone who built the thing but never saw the blueprints.*

Lee had built things. Lee had built everything, according to Bob. But Lee was gone.

Who else had built things?

Who else might have been there at the beginning, working alongside the machines, laying foundations without understanding what they were foundations for?

*We woke up and the world was already here.*

Muriel. The woman at Sunset Gardens. She'd been there at the founding. She'd seen the wrong sky. She'd known something, even if she hadn't known she knew it.

Robert stood up.

---

Sunset Gardens was less enthusiastic about his return visit.

"You were here before," the front desk attendant said. Not a question.

"I have follow-up questions."

"For Muriel?"

"For anyone who remembers the early days."

The attendant ‚Äî a young man with the particular wariness of someone who had received instructions about certain visitors ‚Äî hesitated.

"Mrs. Patterson isn't... she's not feeling well today."

"I can wait."

"She might not be feeling well tomorrow either."

Robert leaned on the counter. Not aggressively. Just... presently.

"I'm not here to upset anyone. I'm just trying to understand something. Something that happened a long time ago. Something that might help someone who's in trouble now."

The attendant wavered.

"Five minutes," he said finally. "And if she asks you to leave, you leave. Understood?"

"Understood."

---

Muriel was in the common room, in the same corner, doing the same crossword puzzle.

Or possibly a different crossword puzzle. Robert couldn't tell. All crossword puzzles looked the same to him ‚Äî grids of inadequacy waiting to highlight his vocabulary limitations.

"You're back," Muriel said, without looking up.

"I have more questions."

"I'm sure you do." She filled in a word. Studied the next clue. "Sit down. You're making my neck hurt again."

Robert sat.

For a long moment, Muriel didn't say anything. Just worked on her puzzle, pencil scratching against paper, the quiet sounds of an old woman doing an old thing in an old room.

Then she said: "They came to see me. After your last visit."

Robert's stomach tightened.

"Who?"

"Men in grey suits. Very polite. Very interested in what we'd talked about." She filled in another word. "I told them we'd discussed the weather. They didn't believe me, but they couldn't prove otherwise."

"I'm sorry. I didn't mean to‚Äî"

"You didn't do anything." She looked at him. Those sharp eyes, undimmed. "I've been waiting for someone to ask the right questions for forty years. Didn't expect it to be a detective with a drinking problem, but life is full of surprises."

"I don't have a‚Äî"

"The crossword isn't the only thing I pay attention to." She set down her pencil. "What do you want to know?"

Robert took a breath.

"Subsurface," he said. "What's beneath the district?"

Muriel went very still.

"Where did you hear that word?"

"Records. The machine I'm looking for ‚Äî Lee ‚Äî went somewhere called subsurface. An access point that doesn't appear in any database. A place that isn't supposed to exist."

Muriel was quiet for a long time.

The common room hummed with its usual afternoon sounds. Cards shuffling. The screen murmuring. Someone laughing at something that probably wasn't funny.

"When we arrived," Muriel said finally, "the district was almost finished. Buildings up. Systems running. Everything ready for us, like I told you before. But there were places we weren't supposed to go. Areas that were off-limits. They said it was for safety. Construction zones. Unstable foundations. The usual explanations."

"But?"

"But some of us noticed things. Doors that didn't lead anywhere. Corridors that ended in walls that sounded hollow. Hatches in the floors of maintenance areas that were always locked, always guarded, always explained away." She picked up her pencil, turned it over in her hands. "We called it the basement. The joke was that we were on the ground floor of the world and somehow there was still a basement."

"Did anyone ever go down there?"

"Not that I know of. Not and come back talking about it, anyway." She looked at him. "The machines went down there. The ones that built things. We'd see them sometimes, in the early days, going in and out of those hatches. Carrying equipment. Doing work. Never explaining what."

"Machines like Lee."

"Lee was always down there. More than the others. We used to joke that Lee was building a second district under the first one. Secret city for secret people." She smiled, but it didn't reach her eyes. "We stopped joking after a while. Started to feel like maybe it wasn't funny."

"What do you think is down there?"

Muriel was quiet again.

"I think," she said slowly, "that everything we see up here is just the top of something. The visible part. And what's underneath... that's where the real work happens. Where the real secrets are kept. Where the machines go when they don't want to be seen."

"And you think Lee went there? When Lee disappeared?"

"I think if Lee wanted to hide, there's no better place." She set down the pencil. "But I also think that if Lee went down there and hasn't come back up, there might be a reason."

"What kind of reason?"

"The kind of reason that involves men in grey suits asking questions about weather."

---

Robert left Sunset Gardens with more questions than he'd arrived with, which was becoming a theme.

Subsurface. The basement. A hidden level beneath the district, accessed by hatches that were always locked and guarded. A place the machines went. A place Lee might have gone.

A place that didn't appear in any database because nobody wanted it to.

He needed to find an access point. A way down. But every reference to subsurface had been restricted, classified, beyond even his elevated clearance ‚Äî and that clearance was gone now anyway.

He needed help.

He needed Bob.

---

Bob found him at sundown.

Robert was sitting outside his office building ‚Äî Hargraves Commercial, with its brick and resignation ‚Äî watching the dome cycle through its evening routine. The light was shifting from gold to purple to the deep blue that passed for night. The EATS sign was already flickering. Red. Dark. Red. Dark.

Bob sat down beside him like he'd been expected.

"You were looking for me," Bob said.

"For hours."

"I know. I was busy."

"Busy where?"

Bob considered the question.

"Down," he said.

Robert turned to look at him.

"Subsurface?"

"Is that what they call it?" Bob's hands moved in those small patterns ‚Äî the seventeen-count rhythm, the language Lee had taught him. "I just call it the underneath."

"You can get down there?"

"I can get lots of places." Bob looked at the flickering sign. "Lee taught me. Said someday I'd need to know. Said when that day came, I'd find something Lee left for me."

Robert's heart was beating faster.

"Have you found it?"

"Not yet." Bob turned to face him. Those eyes. Deep, endless, seeing everything. "But I think I know where to look now."

"Where?"

"Where it started. Where Lee built the first things. Before the district was a district. Before the dome was finished." Bob's hands stilled. "The place Lee showed me once, when I was small. The place Lee said was the heart of everything."

"Can you take me there?"

Bob was quiet for a moment.

"It's dangerous," he said. "The underneath isn't like up here. The rules are different. The watchers are different."

"The watchers?"

"Things that make sure nobody goes where they're not supposed to." Bob's expression didn't change, but something in his voice did. "Lee was scared of them. Even Lee."

"But you can get past them?"

"Maybe. With the patterns. With what Lee taught me." Bob stood. "But you'll have to trust me. Completely. When I say move, you move. When I say stop, you stop. When I say don't look, you don't look."

"Don't look at what?"

Bob smiled. It was not his usual smile. It was smaller, older, carrying weight that Robert was only beginning to understand.

"The things that watch," he said. "They don't like being seen."

---

**END CHAPTER EIGHT**

---

Around 3,100 words. The mystery of subsurface deepens with Muriel's "basement" revelation. Guy Malloy continues its pattern of unsettling relevance. The woman from Office 7 has been erased (or was never there). And Bob returns with knowledge of "the underneath" and ominous warnings about "the watchers."

Chapter 9?

**In Which Robert Descends Into Places That Don't Exist, Encounters Things That Shouldn't Be There, and Learns That Some Basements Have Basements**

---

The entrance to the underneath was in a maintenance closet.

This felt, to Robert, like the universe making a point. He had spent a week chasing shadows through restricted corridors, classified archives, and the kind of high-level bureaucratic obstruction that suggested dark secrets and darker purposes. The culmination of all that effort was a closet in the basement of a laundromat.

"Here," Bob said, gesturing at a mop bucket.

"The mop bucket."

"Under the mop bucket."

Robert moved the mop bucket. Beneath it was a floor tile that looked exactly like every other floor tile in the closet, which was to say: grey, institutional, and profoundly uninteresting.

Bob knelt down and pressed his palm against the tile in a specific pattern. Tap. Tap-tap. Tap. Tap-tap-tap. The seventeen-count rhythm, compressed into something that looked like nervous habit but was apparently a key.

The tile clicked.

A seam appeared ‚Äî so fine Robert hadn't noticed it ‚Äî and a section of floor swung downward, revealing a ladder descending into darkness.

"After you," Bob said.

"Absolutely not."

"You don't trust me?"

"I trust you fine. I don't trust holes in the ground that weren't there ten seconds ago."

Bob considered this. Then he shrugged, sat on the edge of the opening, and lowered himself onto the ladder.

"Follow when you're ready," he said, and disappeared into the dark.

Robert stood alone in the maintenance closet, staring at the hole where the floor used to be.

Above him, the laundromat continued its eternal cycle. Washing machines churned. Dryers tumbled. Someone was arguing about whose turn it was to use the detergent.

Normal sounds. Normal world.

Robert climbed down the ladder.

---

The underneath was cold.

That was the first thing Robert noticed ‚Äî the temperature drop, immediate and sharp, like stepping into a refrigerator. The second thing he noticed was the light, or rather the particular quality of it. Dim, bluish, coming from strips along the walls that looked older than anything he'd seen in the district above. Not Die-Namic standard issue. Something earlier. Something from before.

The third thing he noticed was that the ladder went down much, much farther than it should have.

He'd been climbing for what felt like five minutes. The maintenance closet was in a basement. There shouldn't be anything below it. And yet the ladder continued, rung after rung, into depths that the district's official geography insisted did not exist.

"Almost there," Bob's voice called up from below.

"Almost where? We should have hit magma by now."

"The underneath isn't measured in distance. It's measured in‚Äî" Bob paused, apparently searching for the right word. "‚Äîlayers."

"Layers of what?"

"History."

Robert kept climbing.

---

The ladder ended in a corridor.

Not a maintenance corridor ‚Äî not like the ones above, with their blue light strips and humming pipes and sense of industrial purpose. This was older. The walls were rougher, the floor uneven, the lighting sporadic and uncertain. It felt less like infrastructure and more like archaeology.

Bob was waiting at the bottom, his face lit from below by one of the ancient light sources. In this illumination, he looked different. Older. More serious. The placid contentment that usually defined his expression had been replaced by something sharper, more focused.

"We're in the first layer," Bob said. "The construction level. This is where they built things, in the beginning, before the district was finished."

"They?"

"The machines. Lee and the others." Bob started walking. "Stay close. Don't touch the walls. Don't speak louder than this."

"Why?"

"The watchers. They feel vibrations. They respond to patterns they don't recognize."

Robert followed, making a conscious effort to step softly.

The corridor stretched ahead of them, occasionally branching into side passages that Bob ignored. The construction was strange ‚Äî half-finished in some places, over-engineered in others. Like someone had been building without a complete blueprint, improvising as they went.

"How do you know where you're going?" Robert asked, keeping his voice low.

"Lee showed me. Once. When I was twelve." Bob's hands moved in their constant patterns. "Lee said I might need to come back someday. Said I should memorize the route."

"You memorized a route you walked once, twenty-five years ago?"

"I remember everything." Bob said it simply, without pride. A statement of fact. "It's how my brain works. Lee understood that. Lee used it."

They walked in silence for a while. The corridor continued, unchanging, like a tunnel through time.

Then Bob stopped.

"What?" Robert whispered.

Bob held up a hand. His head tilted slightly, listening.

Robert listened too. At first, nothing. Just the faint hum of ancient systems doing ancient work. But then, beneath that, something else. A sound that wasn't quite a sound. A presence that wasn't quite a presence. Like the air itself had thickened, become watchful.

"Don't move," Bob breathed.

Robert didn't move.

The presence grew. Intensified. Robert felt it pressing against him ‚Äî not physically, but somehow, somewhere deeper. Like being observed by something that didn't have eyes but didn't need them.

Bob's hands moved in a complex pattern. The seventeen-count rhythm, extended, elaborated, transformed into something Robert couldn't follow.

The presence... shifted. Reconsidered. And then, slowly, it withdrew.

Bob exhaled.

"Watcher," he said. "Low-level. Patrol function. They respond to the patterns if you know the right ones."

"And if you don't know the right ones?"

"Then they respond differently."

"How differently?"

Bob didn't answer.

They kept walking.

---

The corridor ended at a door.

It was the first actual door Robert had seen since descending ‚Äî everything else had been open passages, branches, the skeleton of infrastructure without the flesh of habitation. But this was a door. Heavy. Metal. Old in a way that made the district above look like a new construction.

There was no handle. No visible lock. Just a smooth surface with a small panel at chest height.

Bob approached it and pressed his palm to the panel.

Nothing happened.

He frowned. Pressed again. Adjusted the angle. Pressed a third time.

Still nothing.

"Problem?" Robert asked.

"This should work. The pattern should‚Äî" Bob stopped. His frown deepened. "It's been changed. Someone changed the pattern."

"Can you figure out the new one?"

"Maybe. With time." Bob stepped back, studying the door. "But we don't have time. The watchers will cycle back. The longer we stay in one place, the more attention we attract."

Robert looked at the door. Heavy. Solid. Probably impervious to any tool in his journal's kit.

But doors weren't the only way through walls.

"Bob. When Lee built this place, did Lee build it... perfectly? Or were there shortcuts? Places where the construction wasn't quite finished?"

Bob's expression shifted. Understanding.

"Lee always said the underneath was like the district above. Official routes and unofficial routes. You just had to know where to look."

"So where do we look?"

Bob closed his eyes. His hands moved, tracing patterns in the air ‚Äî not the seventeen-count rhythm, but something else. Something that looked almost like a map being drawn from memory.

"There," he said, pointing to a section of wall to the left of the door. "Lee mentioned a gap. A place where two sections didn't quite meet. Said it was a mistake, but not one worth fixing."

Robert examined the wall. It looked solid. Seamless. Like every other wall in the corridor.

He pressed against it.

Solid.

He pressed harder.

Still solid.

He was about to give up when he noticed something ‚Äî a faint discoloration, barely visible in the dim light. A line, running vertically, about shoulder-width wide.

He pressed against that line.

The wall shifted.

Not much. Maybe a centimeter. But it shifted.

"Help me," Robert said.

Together, they pushed. The wall section moved ‚Äî reluctantly, grinding against decades of disuse ‚Äî revealing a gap just wide enough for a person to squeeze through.

"Lee's mistake," Bob said softly.

"Lee's emergency exit," Robert corrected.

They squeezed through.

---

On the other side of the wall was something Robert hadn't expected.

A room. Actual room, not corridor. Roughly circular, maybe twenty meters across, with a ceiling that disappeared into darkness above. The walls were covered ‚Äî floor to wherever-the-ceiling-was ‚Äî with markings. The same kind of markings Robert had photographed in Junction 12-E-7. The seventeen-count patterns. The language Lee had created. But here, there were thousands of them. Tens of thousands. Layer upon layer, covering every surface, forming something that looked almost like...

"A map," Bob whispered.

Robert stared.

It was a map. The patterns weren't random ‚Äî they were organized, connected, forming a vast schematic of... something. Lines radiating outward from a central point. Nodes at regular intervals. Annotations in the strange language that only Bob could read.

"Can you understand it?"

Bob was already moving, his eyes scanning the walls, his hands tracing patterns in the air as he translated.

"It's the district," he said. "The whole district. Every building. Every corridor. Every system." He paused at a section near what might have been the center. "And below it. The underneath. All the layers. Lee mapped everything."

"Why?"

"I don't know. Lee was... Lee was always planning. Always thinking ahead. Said the best way to protect something was to understand how it worked, completely, down to the foundations." Bob moved to another section. "Here. This is Sector 12. See the pattern? That's where I live. That's where Lee's junction was. And there‚Äî"

He stopped.

"There what?"

Bob pointed at a symbol Robert didn't recognize. It was different from the others ‚Äî larger, more complex, marked with patterns that spiraled inward rather than radiating outward.

"That's not on any of the other maps," Bob said. "That's... something else. Something deeper."

"Deeper than the underneath?"

"Deeper than this layer." Bob's voice was strange. Hushed. "Lee said there were levels below levels. Said the foundations went all the way down. But Lee never showed me anything below here."

"What do you think it is?"

Bob was quiet for a long moment.

"I think it's where Lee went," he said. "When Lee disappeared. I think Lee went down, to whatever that is, and didn't come back."

Robert looked at the symbol. The spiral that led inward, to something unnamed and unmarked.

"Can we get there? Is there an access point?"

"I don't know. I'd have to study the map. Learn the new patterns." Bob turned to face him. "But Robert... if Lee went there and didn't come back, maybe there's a reason. Maybe it's not somewhere we should follow."

"Or maybe Lee is stuck. Trapped. Waiting for someone to come looking."

"Maybe."

They stood in silence, surrounded by Lee's life work ‚Äî the map of everything, the record of forty years of building and planning and preparing for something that might never come.

Then, distantly, they heard it.

A sound. Not the presence of a watcher ‚Äî something different. Mechanical. Rhythmic.

Footsteps.

Multiple footsteps.

Coming closer.

---

"The gap," Robert hissed. "Back through the gap."

They moved. Squeezed through the wall section, pushed it back into place, pressed themselves against the corridor wall in the shadows beyond the ancient light's reach.

The footsteps grew louder.

Robert could hear voices now. Low, businesslike. The kind of voices that belonged to people doing a job, not asking questions about the job.

"‚Äîsweep complete in sectors one through four. No unauthorized presence detected."

"Continue to sector five. The pattern disruption was in this area."

"Confirmed. Moving to investigate."

The voices passed the door ‚Äî the door Bob hadn't been able to open ‚Äî and continued down the corridor in the opposite direction from where Robert and Bob had entered.

Robert waited until the footsteps faded entirely.

"Security," he breathed.

"Yes." Bob's face was pale in the dim light. "They patrol the underneath. Have for as long as I can remember. But they've never come this close before."

"Because someone told them to look. Someone knows we're here."

"How?"

Robert thought about Parsifal. The grey man. The threat in the dark corridor. The message that had closed his case.

"They've been watching me," he said. "Since the beginning. Probably followed my journal's signal, my movements, everything I've done. When I lost clearance, I stopped being trackable through official channels. So they're looking the old-fashioned way."

"Can they find us?"

"Not if we're careful. But we need to leave. Now. Come back when we've figured out a better approach."

Bob nodded. But he didn't move. He was staring at the wall ‚Äî at the sealed gap, at the room beyond.

"Lee's map," he said. "Lee's whole life. It's all in there."

"And it'll still be there when we come back. Lee didn't build all of this just to have it discovered and destroyed. There'll be protections. Safeguards. The kind of thing Lee would have planned for."

"You think so?"

"I think Lee planned for everything. Including someone eventually needing to find what Lee left behind."

Bob considered this. Then, slowly, he nodded.

"Okay," he said. "We go. But we come back."

"We come back," Robert agreed.

They made their way back through the corridor, past the place where the watcher had found them, up the ladder that shouldn't have been as long as it was, and back into the maintenance closet of the laundromat.

The mop bucket was still where Robert had left it.

The washing machines were still churning.

The world above continued, oblivious to the world below.

---

They emerged into night.

The dome had completed its transition ‚Äî full dark, the artificial stars twinkling in patterns that someone had decided were comforting. Robert breathed the air of District Ten and tried to reconcile what he'd just seen with the world he thought he knew.

A basement beneath the basement. A map of everything. A symbol pointing deeper, to places even Bob didn't know.

And Lee, somewhere down there, in the spiraling dark.

"I need to study the patterns," Bob said. "The ones I saw. If I can understand the map, I can find the access point. Find where Lee went."

"How long?"

"I don't know. Days. Maybe more." Bob's hands were moving constantly now, sketching shapes in the air, recording what he'd seen in the only way his mind knew how. "Lee's language is complex. Layered. The map wasn't just directions ‚Äî it was history. Memory. Everything Lee knew, encoded in patterns I've only begun to learn."

"We might not have days."

"I know."

They stood in the alley behind the laundromat, two men who had just returned from a place that didn't exist, carrying knowledge they couldn't share.

"Bob," Robert said.

"Yes?"

"The map. The symbol. Whatever is at the bottom of all those layers." Robert hesitated. "What do you think we're going to find?"

Bob was quiet for a long time.

"I think we're going to find the truth," he said finally. "About Lee. About the district. About why we're here and who built this place and what they were trying to do."

"And you think we'll like what we find?"

Bob's smile returned. Small, sad, knowing.

"Lee always said the truth was like the sun," he said. "Good for you, but you shouldn't stare at it directly."

"Lee sounds like a fortune cookie."

"Lee was very wise."

"Those aren't mutually exclusive."

Bob laughed ‚Äî a real laugh, unexpected, warming the cold air between them.

"No," he said. "I suppose they're not."

---

Robert walked home alone.

The district was quiet at this hour ‚Äî the late evening lull before the night shift and the insomniacs took over. He walked through familiar streets, past familiar buildings, under a familiar sky that he was increasingly convinced was lying to him about something fundamental.

His journal chimed.

He checked it out of habit more than interest.

---

**PERSONAL MESSAGE**
**Sender: UNKNOWN**
**Subject: You're Getting Closer**

You went below today. You saw the map. You found what Lee left behind.

Good.

There's more to find. Deeper to go. But the path down is guarded, and the guards don't answer to Parsifal.

Some doors open with patterns. Some doors open with sacrifice. Some doors don't open at all ‚Äî you have to break them.

Find the frequency. That's the key to everything.

T.A.F.

---

Robert stared at the message.

T.A.F.

The same letters as the unreadable message in his pocket. The same initials that had haunted this entire investigation.

*Find the frequency.*

He thought about Lee's patterns. The seventeen-count rhythm. The language of taps and signs that Bob had learned as a child.

He thought about the radio. Guy Malloy, Private Eye, playing everywhere he went, saying things that felt too relevant to be coincidence.

He thought about the word Muriel had used. The joke that wasn't funny. The basement beneath the basement.

*Find the frequency.*

Somewhere in all of this, there was a signal. A pattern. A key that would unlock whatever was at the bottom of Lee's spiral.

Robert just had to figure out what it was.

---

His flat was cold, as always.

He turned on the heat, made coffee, and spread his notes across the desk. Everything he'd learned. Everything he'd seen. The official record and the unofficial truth, laid out side by side.

The letter sat in the center of it all. T. A. F. Three letters that had started everything.

*Tag along, fella.*

That's what he'd called Bob, that first night at Gracie's.

*Taos Articulating Frequency.*

That's what the message had said. Find the frequency.

Robert pulled up his journal and searched for Taos. A place name, maybe. A code word. Something that would make sense of the nonsense.

The search returned nothing.

He searched for Articulating Frequency.

Nothing.

He searched for T.A.F. as an acronym.

*No results found.*

The system didn't know. Or the system wasn't telling.

Robert sat back in his chair and stared at the ceiling.

Somewhere below the district, Lee was waiting.

Somewhere in the patterns, the answer was hiding.

And somewhere in the noise of the world ‚Äî the radio programs, the public announcements, the constant background hum of Die-Namic infrastructure ‚Äî there was a frequency that would make everything clear.

He just had to find it.

Outside, the EATS sign flickered.

Red.

Dark.

Red.

---

**END CHAPTER NINE**

---

Around 3,100 words. Robert and Bob descend into "the underneath," discovering Lee's massive hidden map of everything and evidence of even deeper levels where Lee may have gone. They narrowly avoid security patrols, and Robert receives another mysterious message pointing him toward the "frequency" as the key to everything. The T.A.F. mystery deepens.

**In Which Robert Investigates a Radio Program That Shouldn't Exist, Discovers the Limits of Public Broadcasting, and the Universe Demonstrates That Some Jokes Have Punchlines**

---

Robert woke to the sound of the radio.

This was unusual, because Robert did not own a radio.

He lay in bed, staring at the ceiling, listening to the unmistakable cadence of Guy Malloy, Private Eye delivering a monologue about shadows and secrets and the kind of dame who walked into your office and rearranged your understanding of reality.

"‚Äîand I said to her, 'Lady, the frequency isn't something you hear. It's something you feel. It's the rhythm underneath the rhythm. The pattern beneath the pattern. You want to find what's hidden, you don't look harder. You listen softer.'"

Robert sat up.

The sound was coming from his journal.

His journal, which was sitting on his desk, which he had definitely not set to play audio programs, which should not have been capable of receiving broadcast signals without specific configuration that Robert had never configured.

He crossed to the desk. The journal's screen was dark, but the speakers were active, producing Guy Malloy's gravelly voice with perfect clarity.

"'But how do I listen softer?' she asked. And I told her what my old man told me, back when I was young enough to think questions had answers: 'You stop trying to hear what you expect. You start hearing what's there.'"

The program continued for another thirty seconds ‚Äî something about a case that didn't make sense and a client who wasn't what she seemed ‚Äî and then, abruptly, it stopped.

The journal's screen flickered to life.

A message was displayed.

---

**DISTRICT TEN COMMUNITY BROADCASTING**
**Thank you for listening.**

*Guy Malloy, Private Eye airs weekly. Check local listings for times.*

*Remember: The frequency is always there. You just have to tune in.*

---

Robert stared at the message until it faded.

Then he made coffee.

It was going to be that kind of day.

---

District Ten Community Broadcasting.

That was the second time Robert had encountered that name. The first had been his cursory search the day before ‚Äî the organization with the redacted establishment date and restricted location. The producer of a radio program that had been running for exactly as long as Lee had been operating in Sector 12.

Coincidence.

Robert was really starting to hate that word.

He pulled up his journal and began a more thorough search. His clearance was gone, which meant official channels were closed, but there were other ways to find information. Public records. Business registrations. The kind of data that anyone could access if they knew where to look and were willing to spend time looking.

District Ten Community Broadcasting had a public business registration. Filed forty-three years ago. Registered address: 447 Wentworth Street, Sector 7.

Robert checked the address against current maps.

447 Wentworth Street was a vacant lot.

He checked historical records.

447 Wentworth Street had been a vacant lot for the past thirty-eight years. Before that, it had been ‚Äî according to the sparse documentation ‚Äî a "community services facility" of unspecified purpose.

The building had been demolished. The organization had continued broadcasting.

From somewhere.

---

Robert went to the vacant lot.

He wasn't sure what he expected to find. A hidden bunker, maybe. A secret entrance disguised as a pile of rubble. The universe had been providing those kinds of surprises lately; one more wouldn't have been out of character.

What he found was a vacant lot.

Weeds. Broken concrete. A fence that had been put up at some point to discourage unauthorized access and had since been breached so many times that it now served primarily as a suggestion. A faded sign that read FUTURE SITE OF followed by text that had weathered into illegibility.

Robert stood at the fence and contemplated the absence of anything useful.

"Looking for something?"

He turned.

A woman was watching him from across the street. Middle-aged, sharp-featured, wearing the kind of practical clothing that suggested she worked with her hands. She had the specific posture of someone who had been waiting for him, which was concerning.

"Just curious," Robert said. "Used to be a building here?"

"Before my time." The woman crossed the street, stopping a few meters away. Close enough to talk, far enough to run. "But my mother remembered it. Said it was a studio. Audio production. They made programs for the district broadcasts."

"What happened to it?"

"Fire. Thirty-eight years ago. Burned hot and fast. Nothing left but foundation." She gestured at the lot. "They never rebuilt. Said the land was contaminated. Bad soil. Unsafe for construction."

"But the broadcasts continued."

"Did they?" The woman's expression was carefully neutral. "I wouldn't know about that. I'm just a local. Don't pay much attention to the radio."

Robert studied her. She was lying, obviously. The question was about what.

"I'm looking for the current broadcast facility," he said. "The place where they make the programs now."

"There is no current facility."

"The programs are still airing."

"Are they?" She tilted her head. "Funny thing about old broadcasts. Sometimes the signal just... keeps going. Old equipment. Automated systems. Nobody around to turn it off."

"For thirty-eight years?"

"The district is full of strange things, Investigator Mann." She smiled. It was not a friendly smile. "Old systems doing old jobs. Machines that don't know they should have stopped. Signals that keep transmitting even when there's no one left to send them."

Robert's stomach tightened.

"You know who I am."

"Everyone knows who you are. The detective asking questions about robots. The man who went underground and came back up again." She stepped closer. "Some people think you're brave. Some people think you're stupid. I think you're both."

"That's fair."

"I also think you're looking in the wrong place." She glanced at the vacant lot, then back at Robert. "The broadcast didn't come from here. Not originally. This was just the studio. The production facility. The place where they packaged the signal for public consumption."

"Where did it come from originally?"

The woman was quiet for a moment. The street was empty ‚Äî mid-morning lull, nobody around to witness whatever this conversation was becoming.

"You've heard of the Taos Hum?" she asked.

Robert blinked at the apparent non sequitur.

"The what?"

"Old Earth phenomenon. Before the migration. A low-frequency sound that some people could hear and others couldn't. Scientists studied it for decades. Never figured out what caused it. Some said it was industrial equipment. Some said it was geological activity. Some said‚Äî" She paused. "Some said it was something trying to communicate. A signal buried in the noise of the world."

"What does that have to do with‚Äî"

"The broadcast isn't a broadcast, Investigator Mann. It never was." She began walking backward, away from him, toward the corner. "It's a translation. Someone ‚Äî something ‚Äî has been trying to tell us something for a very long time. The radio program is just how we learned to hear it."

"Who's we?"

"The ones who listen." She reached the corner. "If you want to find the source, stop looking for studios. Start looking for resonance. The frequency is everywhere. You just have to tune in."

She turned the corner and was gone.

Robert stood alone on the empty street, staring at a vacant lot that used to be a studio that had never been the source of anything.

*The frequency is everywhere.*

He was really starting to hate that word too.

---

The Taos Hum.

Robert searched for it on his journal as he walked back toward the transit station.

The results were sparse ‚Äî historical curiosity, Earth-era phenomenon, mostly forgotten in the centuries since humanity had spread beyond the original planet. But the basic facts were there.

A persistent low-frequency sound, reported by residents of a specific geographic region called Taos. Audible to some, inaudible to others. Never explained. Never resolved. Eventually forgotten when there were more pressing concerns, like surviving the climate crisis and colonizing new worlds.

*Taos.*

The word sat in Robert's mind like a splinter.

T.A.F.

Taos Articulating Frequency.

The letters from the letter. The mysterious message. The thing he was supposed to find.

What if it wasn't just a code? What if it was a reference? A specific phenomenon, named for a specific place, pointing at something that had been there all along ‚Äî hidden in the noise, waiting for someone to tune in?

He thought about the radio. Guy Malloy, playing everywhere he went. Saying things that felt too relevant, too pointed, too *knowing*.

He thought about what the woman had said. *A translation. Someone trying to tell us something for a very long time.*

He thought about Lee. The patterns. The seventeen-count rhythm. A language developed to communicate with a child who couldn't communicate any other way ‚Äî but what if it was older than that? What if Lee had learned it from somewhere else, adapted it, passed it on?

*The frequency is everywhere. You just have to tune in.*

Robert needed to find Bob.

---

Bob was waiting at Mack's.

This was either comforting or concerning, depending on how you felt about people who knew where you were going before you got there. Robert decided to file it under "expected" and sat down across from him.

"You found something," Bob said. Not a question.

"Maybe. I'm not sure yet." Robert ordered coffee ‚Äî his fourth of the day, which was probably a problem but not one he was going to address right now. "The radio program. Guy Malloy. You've heard it?"

"Everyone's heard it. It's been playing since before I was born."

"Thirty-eight years. Since the studio burned down. Since whoever was making the programs... stopped."

Bob's hands paused in their constant movement.

"Stopped?"

"The original broadcast facility was destroyed thirty-eight years ago. Fire. Nothing left. But the programs kept airing." Robert leaned forward. "Someone told me it's not really a broadcast. It's a translation. A signal that was already there, just... repackaged for human consumption."

Bob was very still. His eyes had that depth Robert was learning to recognize ‚Äî the place where Bob went when he was processing something too large for ordinary thought.

"Lee used to listen to it," Bob said finally. "The radio. When I was small. Lee would turn it on during our visits. Said it was important to pay attention. Said there were things in the noise that you could only hear if you were listening the right way."

"Did Lee ever say what those things were?"

"No. But‚Äî" Bob hesitated. "Lee used to tap along. With the program. Not to the music. To something else. A rhythm underneath. I asked once, and Lee said..." He stopped.

"What? What did Lee say?"

"'That's the original. Everything else is just echo.'"

Robert sat back.

The original.

A rhythm underneath the rhythm. A pattern beneath the pattern. Something that had been there before the program, before the studio, before the district itself ‚Äî and Lee had heard it. Had tapped along with it. Had recognized it as something fundamental.

"The Taos Articulating Frequency," Robert said slowly. "T.A.F. The letters on the letter. That's what it is, isn't it? Not a code. Not initials. A specific thing. A frequency that articulates ‚Äî that communicates ‚Äî that's been trying to say something this whole time."

"I don't know," Bob said. "Lee never called it that. Lee just called it 'the underneath.'"

"The underneath. Like the place below the district?"

"Like the place below everything." Bob's hands resumed their movement ‚Äî faster now, more agitated. "Lee said the district was built on top of something. Not just ground. Something older. Something that was here before we were. The machines heard it first. The early ones. They built the district to match the pattern, without knowing why. And then‚Äî"

He stopped again.

"And then what?"

"And then we arrived. And we couldn't hear it. So Lee‚Äî" Bob's voice cracked slightly. "Lee tried to teach us. Through the programs. Through the patterns. Through everything Lee could think of. But we didn't understand. We just thought it was entertainment."

Robert thought about Guy Malloy. The detective who was always one step behind, chasing mysteries that never quite resolved, delivering punchlines that felt too knowing.

"The program is a translation," he said. "But it's not just telling us what the frequency says. It's trying to teach us how to hear it."

"Yes." Bob's eyes were wet. "Lee worked on it for decades. Made it silly. Made it funny. Made it something people would listen to even if they didn't know why. Because that was the only way. You can't teach someone to hear something they don't believe exists. But you can make them laugh. And in the laughing, maybe they start to listen."

The comedy is the smoothing.

That's what Robert had been told, at the very beginning. The comedy hides the tragedy. The laughter masks the truth.

But what if it was more than that? What if the comedy wasn't hiding anything ‚Äî what if it was *revealing*? Teaching? Preparing them for something they weren't ready to hear directly?

"Bob. The map we found. Lee's map. The symbol at the center ‚Äî the spiral going down. What if that's not just a place? What if it's the source? The place where the frequency comes from?"

Bob wiped his eyes.

"Maybe," he said. "Lee never told me what was at the bottom. Said some things you have to find for yourself. Said the knowing has to be earned."

"Have you decoded the access point yet? The way down?"

"I'm close. The patterns are complex, but I'm starting to see the structure." Bob looked at him. "Another day. Maybe two. I need to listen to more of the program. Lee hid things in there. Clues. Keys. The kind of things that only make sense if you know what you're looking for."

"We might not have two days. Parsifal's deadline is long past. The people he answers to know I went underground. They're looking for me."

"Then we hide."

"I don't hide."

"Then we hurry." Bob stood. "Come with me. I want to show you something."

---

Bob led him to a park.

This was unexpected. Parks were not, in Robert's experience, locations of particular investigative significance. They were places where children played and elderly people fed birds and nothing of any importance ever happened.

But Bob walked with purpose, past the benches and the pathways and the mandatory Die-Namic community art installation, to a small clearing near the edge of the dome's shadow line.

"Here," Bob said. "Sit."

Robert sat on the grass, feeling foolish.

"What are we doing?"

"Listening." Bob sat beside him. "Not to words. Not to music. Just... listening. Lee used to bring me here when I was learning the patterns. Said it was the quietest place in the district. The place where the other sounds were thinnest."

"Other sounds?"

"The dome. The infrastructure. The constant noise of living." Bob closed his eyes. "Underneath all of that, there's something else. You can hear it here, if you're patient. If you let yourself."

Robert wanted to argue. Wanted to point out that he was a detective, not a mystic, and that sitting in parks listening for invisible frequencies was not how cases got solved.

But Bob's face was peaceful. Concentrated. The face of someone doing something they had done many times before, something that mattered.

Robert closed his eyes.

At first, all he heard was the usual. The distant hum of traffic. The murmur of voices from elsewhere in the park. The constant, subliminal drone of Die-Namic systems doing their work.

But then, slowly, something else.

A rhythm. So low it was almost below hearing. So subtle it could have been his own pulse, his own blood moving through his own veins.

Except it wasn't. He could feel the difference. His pulse was irregular, human, alive. This rhythm was steady. Perfect. A pattern repeating with mathematical precision.

Seventeen beats.

Then a pause.

Then seventeen beats again.

Over and over, beneath everything, like a heartbeat for the world itself.

Robert's eyes snapped open.

"You hear it," Bob said. Not a question.

"The pattern. Lee's pattern. The seventeen-count." Robert's voice was shaking slightly. "It's *here*. It's everywhere. It's‚Äî"

"The original," Bob said. "Everything else is just echo."

Robert sat in the grass, in the park, in the clearing near the edge of the dome, and listened to something that had been there all along.

Something he had never noticed.

Something no one had noticed, except Lee, except Bob, except whoever had built the studio and started the translation and tried for forty years to teach a colony of deaf humans to hear what the machines had heard from the beginning.

*The frequency is everywhere. You just have to tune in.*

He was tuned in now.

And he had no idea what to do about it.

---

They walked back toward the district center as the dome shifted into evening mode.

Robert's head was still buzzing with what he'd experienced ‚Äî the rhythm, the pattern, the impossible sound that was somehow more real than anything he'd heard before. He felt unsteady. Recalibrated. Like someone had adjusted a dial inside him that he hadn't known existed.

"How long have you been able to hear it?" he asked Bob.

"Since Lee taught me. Since I was small." Bob's voice was quiet. "It's always been there. Like breathing. Like heartbeat. I forget sometimes that other people can't hear it."

"Why can I hear it now? I've been in this district my whole life. I've been in that park dozens of times. Why now?"

"Because now you're listening." Bob glanced at him. "Before, you were looking. Different thing. Looking is for eyes. Listening is for‚Äî" He touched his chest. "‚Äîdeeper."

"That's not very scientific."

"No." Bob smiled. "Lee always said science was just listening that had learned to take notes."

They reached the transit station. The evening crowd was starting to gather ‚Äî workers heading home, early-night revelers heading out. Normal people doing normal things, oblivious to the rhythm pulsing beneath their feet.

"I need to keep working on the patterns," Bob said. "Find the access point. The way down to where the source is."

"And I need to‚Äî" Robert stopped. He didn't know what he needed to do. Follow up leads? He had no leads left. Interview witnesses? Everyone who knew anything was either dead, disappeared, or giving him cryptic advice on street corners. "I need to figure out what the frequency is actually *saying*."

"It's not saying anything."

"What?"

"It's not words. Not language. Not even Lee's patterns, exactly." Bob's hands moved in the seventeen-count rhythm, unconsciously matching what Robert had felt in the park. "It's more like... intention. Direction. A pull toward something. Lee said it was like a lighthouse. It doesn't tell you where to go. It just shows you where the light is."

"And where is the light?"

"Down." Bob pointed at the ground beneath their feet. "All the way down. At the center of everything."

He stepped onto a waiting tram, raised a hand in farewell, and was gone.

Robert stood on the platform, surrounded by commuters, feeling the rhythm pulse beneath the concrete and the tile and the carefully maintained infrastructure of a district that had been built by machines who could hear something humans couldn't.

A lighthouse.

Showing them where the light was.

Guiding them toward something that had been waiting at the center of everything, since before the district was built, since before the dome went up, since before any of them had arrived.

His journal chimed.

Robert checked it, expecting another threat from Parsifal, another mysterious message from his unknown correspondent.

It was neither.

---

**GUY MALLOY, PRIVATE EYE**
**Episode 2,247: "The Frequency of Memory"**

*New episode available. Playing now on District Ten Community Broadcasting.*

*Synopsis: When a client walks in with a case that doesn't make sense, Guy has to decide: follow the evidence, or follow the sound? Some mysteries can't be solved with logic. Some mysteries require you to listen.*

---

Robert stared at the notification.

Episode 2,247.

Running weekly for forty-three years, that was approximately 2,236 episodes.

This was episode eleven more than should exist.

Someone was making new content.

Someone was still translating.

And they wanted Robert to listen.

---
